Nafaqah — Maintenance
advanced
10 min
Maintenance Obligation with Difference of Religion: The Rule and the Dispute on Criteria
Explains the principle that maintenance is not obligatory with difference of religion except through marriage or lineage, with examples and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that maintenance is not obligatory with difference of religion except through marriage and lineage, apply it to examples, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the criterion for shared maintenance is closeness/juz'iyya or inheritance.

Core Idea
Maintenance across religions only via marriage or lineage; the 'part' status, not inheritance, determines obligation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key rule: when a Muslim and a non-Muslim are related, the obligation to provide maintenance (nafaqa) is not automatic. It only applies through marriage (zawjiyya) or lineage (wilad). The author then presents proofs and examples, and addresses an important intra-Hanafi debate about how to allocate maintenance when multiple relatives are equally close.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the principle from the matn: maintenance is not obligatory with difference of religion except through marriage or lineage.
This is the foundational rule from Kanz al-Daqaiq.
2
Understand the rationale for marriage
Maintenance for a wife is due because she confines herself for the husband's right, regardless of religion. This is based on the marriage contract.
3
Understand the rationale for lineage
For ascendants and descendants, maintenance is based on juz'iyya: the child is a part of the parent, and the parent is a part of the child. Difference of religion does not sever this connection, except for harbis.
The hadith 'You and your property belong to your father' supports this.
4
Apply the rule to examples
Work through examples: daughter's daughter vs brother (maintenance on the daughter's daughter); Christian son vs Muslim brother (maintenance on the son, inheritance on brother); daughter vs patron (maintenance on daughter). Note that maintenance follows lineage, not inheritance.
The author provides these to illustrate the rule.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
When relatives are equally close (e.g., mother and brother), some narrations from Abu Hanifa and al-Bada'i consider inheritance shares, while the mutun (al-Wiqaya, etc.) follow juz'iyya alone. The author states he has resolved this in his treatise Tahrir al-Nuqul.
The relied-upon position is juz'iyya, not inheritance.

تجب كتاب الطلاق باب النفقة اختلاف الدين إلا بالزوجية والولاد ولا يشارك الأب والولد في نفقة ولده مع لأن البنت أقرب، وإذا كان له بنت بنت أو ابن بنت وأخ لأب وأم فالنفقة على ولد البنت ذكراً كان أو أنثى وإن كان الميراث للأخ لا لولد البنت ولو كان له والد وولد موسران فالنفقة على ولده، وإن استويا في القرب لترجح الولد بتأويل أنت ومالك لأبيك. ولو كان له جد وابن ابن فالنفقة عليهما على قدر ميراثهما على الجد السدس والباقي على ابن الابن، والدليل على عدم اعتبار الميراث في هذه النفقة أنه لو كان أحدهما ذمياً فالنفقة عليهما، وإن كان الميراث للمسلم منهما . ولو كان للمسلم الفقير ابن نصراني وأخ مسلم فالنفقة على الميراث ا هـ . ثم قال في البدائع أيضاً : وكذلك إذا كان له أم وأخ لأب وأم أو لأب أو ابن أخ لأب وأم أو لأب أو عم لأب وأم أو لأب كانت النفقة عليهما أثلاثاً، ثلثها على الأم، والثلثان على الأخ وابن الأخ والعم ا هـ. أقول: وهذه الفروع كلها تشكل على اعتبارهم القرب والجزئية دون الميراث فإنهم قد اعتبروا فيها الميراث دون القرب والجزئية إذ مقتضى أصلهم المذكور وجوبها على الأم فقط إلا أن يكون هذا على الرواية الأخرى التي تعتبر الإرث كما عزاها القهستاني إلى الإمام حيث قال : ويعتبر القرب والجزئية لا يعتبر الإرث كما هو رواية عنه ا هـ . لكن رواية اعتبار القرب والجزئية عليها المتون كالوقاية والملتقى والتنوير، ثم ظهر لي الجواب عن هذه الإشكالات بتمامها وحررت المسألة بتحرير لم أسبق إليه في رسالة سميتها تحرير النقول في نفقة الفروع والأصول يلزم على كل فقيه طلبها فإنها أزاحت اللبس وأزالت كل حدس قوله : (والدليل على عدم اعتبار الميراث الخ) قال في البدائع : ولو كان له بنت وأخت فالنفقة على البنت لأن الولاد لها، وهذا يدل على أن النفقة لا تعتبر بالميراث لأن الأخت ترث مع البنت ولا نفقة عليها مع البنت ا هـ. وقد ذكر It is obligatory. Book of Divorce, Chapter of Maintenance. Difference of religion except through marriage and lineage, and the father and son are not shared in the maintenance of his child with... because the daughter is closer. And if he has a daughter's daughter or a son's daughter and a full brother, then maintenance is on the child of the daughter, male or female, even if inheritance is for the brother, not for the child of the daughter. And if he has a father and a son both wealthy, maintenance is on his son, even if they are equal in closeness, due to the preponderance of the son by the interpretation 'you and your property belong to your father'. And if he has a grandfather and a son's son, maintenance is on both according to their shares of inheritance: on the grandfather one-sixth and the remainder on the son's son. And the evidence for not considering inheritance in this maintenance is that if one of them were a dhimmi, maintenance is on both, even if inheritance is for the Muslim of them. And if a poor Muslim has a Christian son and a Muslim brother, maintenance is on the son and inheritance is for the brother. And if a poor person has a daughter and a patron of emancipation both wealthy, maintenance is on the daughter even if they are equal in inheritance; thus in al-Dhakhira. And the author made absolute in the grandfather so it includes paternal grandfather and maternal grandfather. It is decided in al-Dhakhira and others; disagreement is reported about maternal grandfather. And he made absolute in the grandmother so it includes grandmother from father's side and grandmother from mother's side. And in al-Walwalijiyya: If the father takes the prescribed maintenance and clothing in advance and they are lost, another is prescribed for him. But if the period passes and they remain, another is not prescribed, unlike the wife in both cases, and we have mentioned the difference in the beginning of the Chapter of Maintenance. His statement: "And it is not obligatory with difference of religion except through marriage and lineage. As for marriage, it is as we mentioned that it is..." meaning because it is a part, even if they are equal in closeness as in al-Qahistani, and this indicates that the preponderance in his statement "even if they are equal in closeness" includes being a part. His statement: "So maintenance is on both" etc. He said in al-Bada'i: because they are equal in kinship and inheritance and there is no preponderance of one over the other from another aspect, so it is on both according to the amount of inheritance. Then he said in al-Bada'i also: Likewise, if he has a mother and a full brother or half-brother, or a son's brother full or half, or a paternal uncle full or half, maintenance is on both in thirds: one-third on the mother and two-thirds on the brother, son's brother, or paternal uncle. I say: All these branches pose a problem on their considering closeness and being a part without inheritance, for they have considered in them inheritance without closeness and being a part, because the implication of their stated principle is obligation on the mother alone, unless this is according to the other narration which considers inheritance, as al-Qahistani attributed to the Imam when he said: closeness and being a part are considered, inheritance is not considered, as is a narration from him. But the narration of considering closeness and being a part is what the texts like al-Wiqaya, al-Multaqa, and al-Tanwir are upon. Then the answer to all these problems became clear to me, and I researched the issue with a research I was not preceded in, in a treatise I titled 'Tahrir al-Nuqul fi Nafaqat al-Furu' wa al-Usul', which every jurist should seek, for it removes confusion and eliminates all guesswork. His statement: "And the evidence for not considering inheritance" etc. He said in al-Bada'i: If he has a daughter and a sister, maintenance is on the daughter because lineage is for her, and this indicates that maintenance is not based on inheritance, because the sister inherits with the daughter yet there is no maintenance on her with the daughter. This branch is also mentioned in al-Dhakhira, but the author omitted it for brevity. His statement: "And he made absolute in the grandfather" etc. He said in al-Dhakhira: In the case of the grandfather, for entitlement to maintenance, poverty only is considered according to the apparent narration, as in the case of the father. And the grandfather from the mother's side is like the grandfather from the father's side. And for grandmothers, what is considered for grandfathers is also considered.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the principle from Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding maintenance (nafaqa) when there is a difference of religion (ikhtilaf al-din) between the obligated and the beneficiary. The matn states that maintenance is not obligatory with difference of religion except through marriage (zawjiyya) or lineage (wilad). The commentator, Ibn Nujaym, expands on this ruling by presenting proof and intra-Hanafi discussions. He explains that for marriage, the maintenance is due due to the wife confining herself for the husband's right, which does not depend on unity of religion. For lineage, the basis is that a person's offspring and parents are considered a part (juz') of him; just as his own maintenance is not prevented by his disbelief, so too the maintenance of his part is not prevented. However, this applies only to peaceful non-Muslims (dhimmis), not to harbis (enemies at war) because we are prohibited from doing good to those who fight us in religion. The passage then provides examples to illustrate that closeness (qurb) and being a part (juz'iyya) are the criteria, not inheritance (mirath). For instance, if a person has a daughter's daughter and a brother, the maintenance is on the daughter's daughter (the closer in lineage) even though the brother inherits. Similarly, if a poor Muslim has a Christian son and a Muslim brother, maintenance is on the Christian son despite the difference in religion, because the son is a part of the father. The inheritance, however, goes to the Muslim brother. The text also discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school: whether to consider closeness/juz'iyya alone or also inheritance when multiple relatives are equally close. The commentator notes that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that closeness and juz'iyya are the basis, as per al-Wiqaya, al-Multaqa, and al-Tanwir, but al-Bada'i appears to consider inheritance in some cases. Ibn Nujaym states he has resolved these discrepancies in his treatise 'Tahrir al-Nuqul'. He also addresses the grandfather's and grandmother's entitlement, noting that poverty alone is the condition, and that the grandfather from the mother's side is treated similarly to the paternal grandfather. This passage underscores the Hanafi principle that maintenance among relatives is based on the connection of being a part (juz'iyya) of the person, not on the right of inheritance, and that difference of religion does not negate this obligation except for those who are enemies at war.

Key Terms
اختلاف الدين (ikhtilaf al-din): Difference of religion between the person obligated for maintenance and the potential beneficiary, e.g., one is Muslim and the other is non-Muslim.
زوجية (zawjiyya): The marital relationship; being a spouse.
ولاد (wilad): Lineage; being a child (or parent) by birth, including parents, children, and grandchildren.
جزئية (juz'iyya): The quality of being a part (juz') of a person; e.g., a child is considered a part of the parent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is true about maintenance with difference of religion?
A) It is obligatory for all relatives regardless of religion.
B) It is only obligatory through marriage and lineage.
C) It is never obligatory between Muslims and non-Muslims.
D) It is obligatory only through marriage, not lineage.

Key Takeaway
Maintenance between Muslims and non-Muslims is only obligatory through marriage or lineage, based on juz'iyya, not inheritance.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
10 min
No Sharing in Maintenance of Father and Son
Rule and proof for exclusive maintenance obligation of father and son

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that no one shares with the father or son in maintaining their immediate descendants/ascendants and explain the proof from the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father'.

Core Idea
Maintenance of a child is solely on the father, and of parents solely on the son – no other relative is obligated to share.

This lesson examines a foundational principle of Hanafi family maintenance law: the exclusivity of the obligation between fathers and children. While other schools may allow sharing with close relatives, the Hanafi school, relying on a prophetic hadith, maintains that the father is uniquely responsible for his child and the son for his parents. We will explore the textual proof, the scope of the rule, and a key intra-madhhab difference regarding adult children.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the maintenance (nafaqah) of a child by his father, and of parents by their son. The ruling is that it is wajib (obligatory) on the designated person, and no one else is obligated to share this duty.
The hukm level is wajib, not fard, because the evidence is zanni in transmission (ahad hadith).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith 'أنت ومالك لأبيك' (You and your property belong to your father). This gives the father a ta'wil (authority) over the son's property, making the son primarily responsible for the father's maintenance and also explaining the exclusivity of the father's own obligation.
The hadith is ahad, thus zanni al-thubut, which affects the hukm level (wajib rather than fard).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'you and your property belong to your father' indicates ownership-like authority. In Hanafi usul, such authority implies that the father's claim on the son's property is exclusive, so no other relative can share in the obligation of maintaining the father. Similarly, by analogy, the son's obligation for his own child is exclusive.
The hadith is used both directly (for the son's obligation to father) and by analogy (for the father's obligation to son).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi difference regarding adult children. Al-Khassaf holds that for adult children, both father and mother are responsible in thirds. However, the majority (al-Mabsut) and the author of the matn maintain that the son alone is responsible, even for adult children.
This ikhtilaf is discussed in the text; the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the majority view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentator (al-Ramli) supports the view of al-Mabsut as the apparent (zahir) position. The author of the matn also sides with the majority, so the mufta bihi is that no one shares with the son in maintaining his parents, including adult children.
The tarjih authority is al-Ramli and the author's own commentary.

And his parents one, and for a close relative who is forbidden (in marriage) poor, unable to earn, according to the amount of inheritance if wealthy, and a valid sale. It is obligatory for her by the contract due to her confinement for a intended right, and this is not related to unity of religion. As for others, because juz'iyya is established and a part of a person is in the meaning of himself, so just as his own maintenance is not prevented by his disbelief, so too the maintenance of his part is not prevented, except if they are harbis, it is not obligatory on the Muslim even if they are equal, because we have been forbidden from doing good to those who fight us in religion. He made absolute in lineage so it includes parents, grandparents, children, and children's children. And in al-Mustasfa: its example: a dhimmi man married a dhimmi woman and they had a child, then the dhimmi woman became Muslim, the child is judged to be Muslim following her, and maintenance is on the father. This is before the child's Islam, and it is possible that he believes in disbelief in his childhood and his disbelief is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And he restricted by marriage and lineage, because beyond that it is not obligatory with difference of religion. So it is not obligatory on a Muslim to maintain his Christian brother, nor the opposite, because maintenance is related to inheritance by text, unlike emancipation upon ownership because it is related to kinship and being a mahram by hadith, and because kinship necessitates connection, and with agreement in religion it is strengthened, and the permanence of right hand ownership is higher in donation than deprivation of maintenance, so we considered in the origin the original cause, and in the lesser the emphasized cause, thus they differ. His statement: "And no one shares with the father and son in the maintenance of his child and his parents." As for the maintenance of the child, we have preceded it. And as for the maintenance of the parents, because they have an interpretation in the son's property by text, and they have no interpretation in the property of others, and because he is the closest person to them, so he is most deserving of their maintenance being upon him. He made absolute in the father so it includes the wealthy and the poor, but in al-Dhakhira: if the father is poor and the mother is wealthy, she is ordered to spend from her wealth on the child, and it becomes a debt that she can recover from him when he becomes wealthy, because the maintenance of the minor is on the father, even if he is poor like his own maintenance. So the mother fulfills an obligatory right on him by order of the judge, and she recovers from him when he becomes wealthy. Then he made the mother more deserving of bearing than other relatives, so that even if the father is poor and the mother is wealthy and the minor has a wealthy grandfather, the mother is ordered to spend from her own wealth and then recover from the father, and the grandfather is not ordered because she is closer. His statement: "And in al-Mustasfa: its example" etc. Al-Ramli said: It is not limited to this example, because in lineage relationship if the father or son is disabled or blind or similar, and he cannot earn in any way, and with difference of religion with the child, if the older person becomes Muslim in lineage relationship and has one of these descriptions, his maintenance is obligatory as a debt, and the absolute text includes him like others. And in al-Dhakhira al-Burhaniyya: A Muslim is not compelled to support a disbelieving relative, nor a disbeliever to support a Muslim relative, except the spouse, parents, and child. The absolute in 'child' includes the minor and those whose maintenance is obligatory due to such descriptions, so consider. His statement: "And no one shares with the father and the son in the maintenance of his child and his parents." Al-Ramli said: He made it absolute so it includes the adult child, and it is the answer of al-Mabsut, and it is apparent as he will mention at the end of the discussion. But according to what al-Khassaf mentioned, it is obligatory on the father and mother for adults in thirds. I say: The author means by 'father' what includes the grandfather, and by 'son' what includes the son's son. So in al-Bada'i: No one shares with the son in the maintenance of his parents, and likewise in the maintenance of his grandfather and grandmother when the parents are absent, and no one shares with the father in the maintenance of his child.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn 'ولا يشارك الأب والولد في نفقة ولده وأبويه أحد' (No one shares with the father and son in the maintenance of his child and his parents). The commentator first explains the rationale: for the maintenance of a child, the father is solely responsible because the hadith 'أنت ومالك لأبيك' gives the father a special right over the son's property. For the maintenance of parents, the son is solely responsible because he is the closest person to them. No other relative, even if equally close, can be obligated to share in this maintenance. The authority of the father and son is exclusive. The text then discusses a scenario where the father is poor but the mother is wealthy: the mother is ordered to spend on the child from her own wealth, but this is considered a debt she can recover from the father when he becomes wealthy. She is given priority over the grandfather because she is closer. The passage also addresses the case of an adult child: according to al-Mabsut and the apparent view, the adult child is also included in the obligation of the son; however, al-Khassaf holds that for adults, both father and mother are liable in thirds. The commentator notes that the author uses 'father' to include grandfather and 'son' to include grandson. The overall ruling is that the obligation is exclusive: no one else shares with the father or son in their respective primary maintenance duties. The text also reiterates the principle regarding difference of religion: maintenance is only obligatory through marriage or lineage, and not for other relatives like brothers. It also mentions that for harbis, no maintenance is due due to the prohibition of doing good to those who fight us.

Key Terms
لا يشارك (la yusharik): No one else shares; the obligation is exclusive to the designated person.
تأويل (ta'wil): A legal interpretation or basis; here, the hadith gives the father a 'ta'wil' (right or authority) over the son's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a minor child's father is poor but the mother is wealthy, and there is also a wealthy grandfather, who is ordered to spend on the child's maintenance?
A) The grandfather, because he is also a male relative
B) The mother is ordered to spend, and she can recover the debt from the father later
C) The state welfare fund
D) The father, even though he is poor, must find a way

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the obligation to maintain a child is exclusively on the father, and to maintain parents exclusively on the son, based on the hadith 'أنت ومالك لأبيك', and no other relative shares this duty.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Mother Borrowing for Child Maintenance: Conditions of Recourse
Procedural expansion on child maintenance: when mother may borrow and recover

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mother may borrow for child maintenance and recover from the father, and apply the rule that court authorization is required for recourse.

Core Idea
A mother may recover borrowed maintenance from the father only if she borrows by court order; otherwise, she has no recourse.

In Hanafi fiqh, the father is obligated to provide maintenance (nafaqah) for his minor children. When a father who is able to pay refuses or absconds, the mother cannot simply spend her own money and then demand reimbursement. Instead, the judge authorizes her to borrow on the father's account. This lesson unpacks the precise conditions for such borrowing and the right of recourse, as well as what happens when the mother does not obtain a court order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The passage begins with the basic case: the father is capable (wajid) but refuses to pay after the judge has fixed maintenance. The judge then orders the mother to borrow and spend; she can recover from the father.
2
Recognize the condition of court authorization
In every scenario described, the mother's borrowing is conditioned on the judge's order. Without it, she may not have the right to recover. This is the central procedural requirement.
The text uses 'أمر القاضي' (order of the judge) repeatedly.
3
Distinguish the three situations of father's failure
The passage lists three situations: (1) father refuses to pay despite ability, (2) father is absent after maintenance was imposed, (3) father is poor (mu'sir) regarding breastfeeding expenses. In all three, the judge orders borrowing, and she recovers when possible.
For the poor father, recovery occurs when he becomes wealthy.
4
Understand the consequence of not borrowing
If the mother does not borrow and the children beg from people, she loses the right to recover because the need was met by charity. If they receive half of sufficient, half of the father's liability drops.
The principle: recourse only if actual borrowing occurred by court order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان الأب واجداً للنفقة لكن امتنع من النفقة على الصغير ففرض القاضي النفقة على الأب فامتنع عن الأداء فالقاضي يأمرها أن تستدين عليه وتنفق على الصغير لترجع بذلك على الأب، وكذلك لو غاب الأب بعد فرض نفقة الأولاد وتركهم بلا نفقة فاستدانت بأمر القاضي وأنفقت عليهم رجعت عليه، وكذلك هذا الحكم في مؤنة الرضاع إذا كان الأب معسراً فالقاضي يأمر الأم بالاستدانة، فإذا أيسر رجعت عليه بالقدر الذي أمرها القاضي بالاستدانة، وإن لم تستدن بعد الفرض لكن كانوا يأكلون من مسألة الناس فلا رجوع لها لوقوع الاستغناء، فإن كانوا أعطوا مقدار نصف الكفاية سقط نصف النفقة عن الأب في النصف الباقي وعلى هذا القياس وكذا في نفقة المحارم وسيأتي تمامه.
And if the father is capable of providing maintenance but refuses to provide for the minor, and the judge imposed maintenance on the father, and he refrained from paying, the judge orders her (the mother) to borrow on his account and spend on the minor, so that she may recover that from the father. Similarly, if the father is absent after the imposition of maintenance for the children and leaves them without maintenance, and she borrows by order of the judge and spends on them, she recovers from him. Likewise, this ruling applies to the expense of breastfeeding if the father is poor: the judge orders the mother to borrow; then when he becomes wealthy, she recovers from him to the extent that the judge ordered her to borrow. And if she did not borrow after the imposition, but they (the children) were eating from begging, then she has no recourse because they became independent. And if they were given an amount equivalent to half of sufficiency, half of the maintenance is dropped from the father for the remaining half, and this is the analogy. Similarly, for the maintenance of relatives, and its completion will come.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a series of rulings in the context of the obligation of child maintenance. It operates as a procedural expansion of the basic rule that the father must provide nafaqah for his minor children. The text first addresses a scenario where the father has the means (wajid) but refuses to pay after the judge has fixed the amount. In such a case, the judge instructs the mother to borrow money on the father's credit and spend it on the child; she then has the right to claim that amount back from the father. This is a practical solution to avoid delay in the child's care. The same ruling applies if the father disappears after the court order. The mother borrows by court order and can recover. The text extends this to the case where the father is poor (mu'sir) specifically for the nursing (rada') expense: the mother borrows by court order and recovers when the father becomes wealthy. However, if the mother does not borrow but the children survive by begging from people, she has no recourse because the need was met by donations. If they received half of what is needed, the father's liability is reduced by half. The author notes that the same principle applies to maintenance of mahram relatives (e.g., parents) and promises further elaboration. The teacher should note that these rulings are based on the principle of court authorization (amr al-qadi) and the right of recourse against the father. The Hanafi school emphasizes the role of the judge in authorizing borrowing to ensure the child's welfare.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, specifically the financial obligation to support dependents including food, clothing, and shelter.
استدانة (istidanah): Borrowing money, in this context with the intention of recovering from the father by court order.
رضاع (rada'): Breastfeeding, the nursing of an infant. Its expense is considered part of nafaqah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the mother have the right to recover from the father for borrowed maintenance?
A) Whenever she spends on the child.
B) Only if she borrows by court order after the father's refusal or absence.
C) Only if the father is wealthy.
D) She can never recover.

Key Takeaway
A mother's right to recover borrowed maintenance from the father is conditional on prior judicial authorization to borrow; without it, the expenditure is a donation and irrecoverable.

Grandfather's Maintenance Obligation When Father is Poor
Examines different views on grandfather's maintenance obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi position on the grandfather's maintenance obligation when the father is poor, distinguish it from the Quduri/Hasan ibn Salih view, and explain the practical consequence.

Core Idea
When a father is poor, the grandfather is obliged to maintain grandchildren as if the father were dead — he cannot recover.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding who bears the maintenance of minor children when the father is poor but a wealthy grandfather exists. The correct view treats the poor father as dead, making the grandfather primarily liable, while an alternative view considers the grandfather's payment as a loan to the father. Understanding this dispute clarifies the practical fatwa on maintenance obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and positions
First, note the two views: al-Quduri (and al-Hasan ibn Salih) hold the grandfather is not obliged; the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq holds the correct view is that the grandfather is obliged, treating the poor father as dead.
Al-Quduri's view is attributed to al-Hasan ibn Salih, a non-Hanafi scholar.
2
Examine the core principle (hanafi dalil)
The author states the poor father 'is attached to the dead' in deserving maintenance from the grandfather. This is the key principle: the father's poverty triggers the same obligation as death.
This principle is found earlier in the chapter.
3
Understand the opposing rationale
Al-Quduri's reasoning: the father is alive, so the obligation remains on him; the grandfather is merely asked to spend voluntarily, and that spending becomes a debt on the father.
This preserves the father's primary liability.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under the correct view, the grandfather cannot recover from the father. Under al-Quduri's view, the father owes the grandfather for amounts spent.
This impacts whether the grandfather has a claim.
5
Recognize the tarjih
The author declares the correct view as 'al-sahih min al-madhhab' and cites al-Sadr al-Shahid. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
The tarjih is based on the principle of the child's welfare.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان للفقير أولاد صغار وجد موسر لم تفرض النفقة على الجد ولكن يؤمر الجد بالإنفاق صيانة لولد الولد ويكون ذلك ديناً على والد الصغار. وهكذا ذكر القدوري فلم يجعل النفقة على الجد حال عسرة الأب، وقد ذكرنا في أول هذا الفصل أن الأب الفقير يلحق بالميت في استحقاق النفقة على الجد وهذا هو الصحيح من المذهب وما ذكره القدوري قول الحسن بن صالح؛ هكذا ذكر الصدر الشهيد في أدب القاضي للخصاف وإن كان الأب زمناً قضى بنفقة الصغار على الجد ولم يرجع على أحد بالإنفاق لأن نفقة الأب في هذه الحالة على الجد وكذا لا يشارك الجد أحد في نفقة ولد ولده عند عدم وليه لقيامه مقامه عند عدمه ا هـ
And if a poor man has small children and a wealthy grandfather exists, the maintenance is not imposed on the grandfather, but the grandfather is ordered to spend out of preservation for the child of this child, and that becomes a debt on the father of the young ones. Thus al-Quduri mentioned, so he did not make maintenance on the grandfather in the case of the father's poverty. And we mentioned at the beginning of this chapter that the poor father is attached to the dead in deserving maintenance from the grandfather, and this is the correct [view] of the madhhab. What al-Quduri mentioned is the statement of al-Hasan ibn Salih; thus al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in Adab al-Qadi li al-Khassaf. And if the father is an invalid (zamin), he decided maintenance of the young ones on the grandfather, and he does not have recourse against anyone for the spending, because the father's maintenance in this case is on the grandfather. Similarly, no one shares with the grandfather in the maintenance of his son's child when there is no guardian for him, because he stands in his place when he is absent. End quote.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage addresses a disputed issue: who bears the maintenance (nafaqah) of minor children when the father is poor (mu'sir) but there is a wealthy grandfather (jadd mawsir)? The text presents two positions. According to al-Quduri, the grandfather is not obliged to maintain; rather he is merely ordered to spend as a moral duty, and that spending becomes a debt on the father. This is also the view attributed to al-Hasan ibn Salih. However, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq declares that the correct view (al-sahih min al-madhhab) is that the poor father is treated like a dead person regarding the grandfather's obligation: just as if the father were dead, the grandfather would be obliged to maintain the grandchildren, so here too the grandfather must maintain. The text further specifies that if the father is both poor and an invalid (zamin, meaning chronically ill or disabled), then the grandfather is obliged to maintain without any recourse against anyone, because the father's own maintenance is on the grandfather. The grandfather acts in loco parentis. The teacher should note that this dispute has practical implications: under the Quduri view, the father remains liable and the grandfather's spending is a loan; under the correct view, the grandfather is primarily liable and cannot recover from the father. The author sides with the latter, citing the authority of al-Sadr al-Shahid.

Key Terms
جد (jadd): Grandfather, specifically the father's father (paternal grandfather).
معسر (mu'sir): Poor, unable to pay maintenance.
زمن (zamin): Invalid, chronically ill or disabled, unable to earn or work.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the passage, what is the grandfather's obligation regarding maintenance of grandchildren when the father is poor?
A) The grandfather is not obliged; he may spend voluntarily.
B) The grandfather is obliged to maintain but can recover from the father.
C) The grandfather is obliged to maintain and cannot recover from the father.
D) The grandfather must pay only if the father is absent.

Key Takeaway
In the correct Hanafi view, the grandfather must maintain grandchildren when the father is poor, and he cannot reclaim from the father.

Abu Yusuf's Hierarchy of Maintenance Liability for a Poor Father
Presents Abu Yusuf's hierarchical liability for minor's maintenance when father is poor and reconciles with standard texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Yusuf's hierarchical order of liability for a minor's maintenance when the father is poor, and explain how this order is reconciled with the standard texts.

Core Idea
The poor father's relatives step in not as partners but as his substitutes; maternal relatives spend only as a loan to preserve the father's exclusive liability.

You have learned that a father is primarily responsible for his child's maintenance. But what if the father is poor and invalid? Abu Yusuf provides a detailed ruling that extends liability to paternal relatives, then to specific maternal relatives, and finally back to the father with a loan mechanism. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents Abu Yusuf's hierarchy and then reconciles it with the standard texts (mutoon) that state 'no one shares with the father'. Understanding this reconciliation is essential for grasping the Hanafi method of handling multiple narrations within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline rule from the mutoon
The standard texts (mutoon) state: 'No one shares with the father in the maintenance of his child.' This implies the father alone is liable, and if he is poor, the mother may borrow on his behalf.
2
State the correction by al-Dhakhirah
According to the author of al-Dhakhirah (al-Sarakhsi), when the father is poor, the grandfather is liable for the minor's maintenance without recourse to the father.
3
Present Abu Yusuf's detailed hierarchy
Abu Yusuf rules: first, liability falls on paternal relatives who would be obliged for the father's own maintenance; if none, then on maternal relatives who are mahram and heirs; if none of these, then on the father, but the maternal relatives are ordered to spend as a loan recoverable from the father.
This hierarchy ensures someone is always responsible.
4
Analyse the reasoning that prevents 'sharing'
Abu Yusuf explains that paternal relatives are like the father – they are obliged for the father's maintenance, so their spending on the child is not sharing but substitution. Maternal relatives' spending is a loan, not a sharing, because the father remains ultimately liable.
5
Apply al-Ramli's reconciliation
Al-Ramli resolves the apparent contradiction by noting that the mutoon are based on a different narration (that of al-Quduri) where the grandfather is not liable. Both positions are valid within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثم جعل الأم أولى بالتحمل من سائر الأقارب الخ قال الرملي : سيأتي أن الأب المعسر كالميت وأنه إذا لم يكن للولد أب وله أم وجد أبو أب كانت النفقة عليهما. فالحاصل أن الأب إذا كان معسراً ففي ذلك خلاف فالمتون أنها على الأب وتستدين الأم وعلى ما صححه صاحب الذخيرة على الجد. وهل يستدين على الأب ويرجع فيه خلاف أيضاً. وأما الأم فتستدين وترجع فتأمل. وفي الصغرى امرأة لها ابن صغير لا مال له ولا للمرأة فاستدانت وأنفقت على الصغير بأمر القاضي فبلغ لا ترجع عليه بذلك؛ كذا في التتارخانية. والمسألة في كثير من الكتب كالبزازية وغيرها. حتى قوله : (وقد ذكرنا أول هذا الفصل قال الرملي : هو من كلام صاحب الذخيرة. شرط وجوب النفقة في غير قرابة الولاد المحرمية وأهلية الإرث، فأما إذا كان في قرابة الأم من كان محرماً للصغير وهو أهل للإرث تجب عليه النفقة ويلحق الأب المعسر بالميت لما ذكرناه اهـ وحاصله أن الوجوب على الأب المعسر إنما هو إذا أنفقت الأم الموسرة وإلا فالأب كالميت والوجوب على غيره لو كان ميتاً ولا رجوع عليه في الصحيح. وعلى هذا فلا بد من إصلاح المتون والشروح كما لا يخفى وأطلق في قوله «في نفقة ولده» أقول : قد علمت أن المخالفة لما في المتون من وجهين : الأول ما إذا كان للفقير أولاد صغار وجد موسر على الجد بلا رجوع في الصحيح على ما في الذخيرة. والثاني ما إذا كان الأب الفقير زمناً فهي على الجد أيضاً بالإنفاق وعلى ما قاله أبو يوسف، فعلى كل من يجبر على نفقة الأب إن كان وإلا فعلى محارم الصغير من قرابة الأم المستحقين للميراث فإن لم يكن أحد من هؤلاء فهي على أبي الصغير لكن يؤمر قرابة الأم بالإنفاق ديناً على الأب فصار الحاصل أن نفقة الصغير إذا كان أبوه معسراً تجب على الجد الموسر تارة، وتارة على غيره من أقارب الأب وتارة على محارمه من قرابة الأم. فهذا كله يخالف المتون في قولهم لا يشارك الأب في نفقة ولده أحد لكن ذكر في كلام أبي يوسف ما يفيد أن قرابة الأب كالأب أو أن المراد جهته وذلك حيث قال بعد قوله السابق «قضيت بالنفقة على أبيه وأمرت قرابة الأم بالانفاق فيكون ديناً على الأب ما نصه : وهذا لأن قرابة الأم لا يجوز أن يجب عليهم نفقة الولد لما عرف أن الأب لا يشارك غيره في نفقة الصغير، فإذا لم يكن للأب قرابة لم يبق هنا وجه سوى أن يقضي بالنفقة على قرابة الأم ويكون ذلك ديناً على الأب لئلا يشارك الأب غيره في نفقة الولد فأما قرابة الأب مما يلزمهم نفقة الأب فجاز أن يلزمهم نفقة الغلام ليكون نفقة ولده جارية مجرى نفقته . هكذا ذكر هذه المسألة في شرح القدوري، وهذا الجواب إنما يستقيم إلى آخر ما ذكره المؤلف، فقد ظهر لك من هذا أن ما وجب على الجد أو غيره من قرابة الأب غير خارج عن قولهم لا يشارك الأب في نفقة ولده بناء على ما قلنا كما هو كالصريح من هذا التعليل. نعم يرد ما أورده صاحب الذخيرة من وجوبها على محارمه من قرابة الأم ولكن بناء على ما عن أبي يوسف فجاز أن يكون ما في المتون جارياً على خلافه. هذا ما ظهر لي والأظهر ما أجاب به الرملي من حمل ما في المتون على الرواية الأخرى يعني ما تقدم عن القدوري من أنها لا تفرض على الجد.
His statement: 'Then he made the mother more deserving of bearing [maintenance] than other relatives' etc. Al-Ramli said: It will come that the poor father is like the dead, and that if the child has no father, and he has a mother and a paternal grandfather, then maintenance is upon both of them. So the conclusion is that when the father is poor, there is a dispute: the standard texts (mutoon) hold that it is upon the father and the mother borrows, while according to what the author of al-Dhakhirah corrected, it is upon the grandfather. And there is also dispute about whether she borrows on the father and recovers. As for the mother, she borrows and recovers, so ponder. And in al-Sughra: a woman has a small son with no wealth, nor does the woman have wealth; she borrowed and spent on the small one by order of the judge, then he became of age – she does not recover from him; thus in al-Tatar Khaniyyah. And the issue appears in many books like al-Bazzaziyyah and others. Until his statement: 'And we mentioned at the beginning of this chapter' – al-Ramli said: It is from the speech of the author of al-Dhakhirah. And his statement: 'Verily the father...' There is no doubt that the poor mother is likewise. And know that he is only attached to the dead according to the one who says so regarding the grandfather – he does not recover. As for regarding the wife, no. And by this their speech in this place is understood, so ponder. End quote. That is, regarding the wife, the poor father is not attached to the dead, because if he were attached to the dead regarding her, it would necessitate that she does not recover, because it is obligatory upon her and upon the grandfather in thirds according to inheritance, originally and not as a substitute for the father. His statement: (a) (h) i.e. the speech of al-Dhakhirah. His statement: 'And the obligation upon others' – by 'others' is meant the grandfather if the young ones have no mother, and the grandfather or others if the father is invalid, i.e. poor, so someone else has shared with the father in spending on his child, so it contradicts the unrestricted nature of the texts. And al-Maqdisi in his commentary answered that the speech of the texts is restricted to ease, because the basic principle for someone obliged to maintenance is that he be wealthy, so ponder. His statement: 'Thus likewise the maintenance of the young ones.' And from Abu Yusuf regarding a small child who has a needy father and he is invalid: his maintenance is imposed on his paternal relatives, not his mother, and everyone who is compelled for the father's maintenance is compelled for the maintenance of the boy. If there is no paternal relative, I decide maintenance on his father and order the maternal relatives to spend, and that becomes a debt on the father. This answer is only correct if among the maternal relatives there is no one who is a mahram of the small child and is eligible for inheritance, because the condition for obligation of maintenance besides the line of birth is mahram relationship and eligibility for inheritance. As for if among the maternal relatives there is a mahram of the small child and he is eligible for inheritance, then maintenance is obligatory upon him, and the poor father is attached to the dead according to what we mentioned. End quote. The summary is that obligation upon the poor father is only if the wealthy mother spends; otherwise the father is like dead and obligation upon others if he were dead, and there is no recourse in the correct view. According to this, the texts and commentaries must be reformed, as is not hidden. And he generalised in his statement 'regarding maintenance of his child' so it included al-Dhakhirah, a completion from... so the maintenance... (and upon this there must be reform of the texts and commentaries. Al-Ramli said: There is no need to reform them because they are based on the second narration, and the authors of texts and commentaries chose it and wrote it in their books, confining themselves to it. End quote. I say: It is known that the contradiction with what is in the texts is in two aspects: first, when the poor has small children and a wealthy grandfather – it is on the grandfather without recourse in the correct view according to al-Dhakhirah. Second, when the father is poor and invalid – it is also on the grandfather for spending, and according to what Abu Yusuf said, it is upon everyone who is compelled for the father's maintenance if there is such a person, otherwise upon the mahrams of the small child from the maternal relatives who are entitled to inheritance. If there is none of these, then it is upon the father of the small child, but the maternal relatives are ordered to spend as a debt on the father. So the conclusion is that the maintenance of the small child when his father is poor is sometimes due upon the wealthy grandfather, sometimes upon others from the father's relatives, and sometimes upon his mahrams from the maternal relatives. All this contradicts the texts in their statement 'No one shares with the father in the maintenance of his child.' But in the speech of Abu Yusuf there is something indicating that the father's relatives are like the father, or that what is meant is his side. That is where he said after his previous statement 'I decided maintenance on his father and ordered the maternal relatives to spend, so it becomes a debt on the father' – his text: 'This is because it is not permissible for the maternal relatives to have the maintenance of the child obligated upon them, because it is known that the father is not shared by anyone in the maintenance of the small child. So if there is no paternal relative for the father, there remains no way except to decide maintenance on the maternal relatives and that becomes a debt on the father, lest the father be shared by others in the maintenance of the child. As for the paternal relatives, since the father's maintenance is obligatory upon them, it is permissible that the maintenance of the boy be obligatory upon them, so that his child's maintenance runs in the course of his own maintenance.' Thus this issue is mentioned in Sharh al-Quduri, and this answer is only valid, up to what the author mentioned. So it has become clear to you that what is obligatory upon the grandfather or others from the paternal relatives is not outside their statement 'no one shares with the father in the maintenance of his child' based on what we said, as is nearly explicit from this reasoning. Yes, what the author of al-Dhakhirah mentioned about obligation upon mahrams from maternal relatives is a problem, but based on what is from Abu Yusuf, it is possible that what is in the texts follows the opposite of that. This is what appeared to me, and the more apparent is what al-Ramli answered by interpreting the texts as based on the other narration, meaning what preceded from al-Quduri that it is not imposed on the grandfather.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This extensive passage from the commentary (sharh) of al-Bahr al-Raiq refines the understanding of whom the obligation of maintenance falls upon when the father is poor (mu'sir) and especially when he is also invalid (zamin). The text begins by referring to a previous statement and then brings in the commentary of al-Ramli, who notes that the poor father is like a dead person in certain respects, and that if the child has a mother and a paternal grandfather (ab al-ab), then maintenance is on both. He then summarises the dispute: the standard texts (mutoon) place the obligation on the father (so the mother borrows), but the author of al-Dhakhirah (Imam al-Sarakhsi) corrected that it is on the grandfather. There is also a dispute about whether the mother can borrow on the father's account and recover. The text then cites a case from al-Sughra (a woman borrows by court order, child reaches maturity, she does not recover from the child). Then al-Ramli explains that the principle of treating the poor father as dead applies only regarding the grandfather (no recourse) but not regarding the wife, otherwise she would not recover. Al-Maqdisi's answer that the texts are restricted to the case of the father being able is mentioned. Then the author brings Abu Yusuf's detailed ruling: for a small child with a needy invalid father, the obligation is first on the paternal relatives; if none, then the judge orders maternal relatives to spend as a debt on the father. But this is only if no maternal relative qualifies as a mahram and heir; if such a relative exists, the obligation falls on him. The author then summarises that the obligation on the poor father is only if the wealthy mother spends; otherwise the father is like dead and the obligation is on others with no recourse. This leads to a discussion on reconciling the mutoon. The author (Ibn al-Humam or similar) explains that the contradiction is only apparent. He identifies two points of contradiction: (1) when father poor with wealthy grandfather – the correct view has grandson's maintenance on grandfather without recourse (contradicting 'no sharing'); (2) when father poor and invalid – maintenance on grandfather or others. But the author argues that the statement 'no one shares with the father' actually means that the father's side (his relatives) are acting in his place, not sharing with him. So when they spend, they are like him, not sharing. Abu Yusuf's reasoning is cited to support this: paternal relatives are like the father because they are obliged for his maintenance, so they are not 'sharing' but rather taking his place. The maternal relatives are only called upon as a last resort, and their spending is a debt, not a sharing. The author then notes that the al-Dhakhirah's case of maternal mahram relatives does cause a problem, but it may be based on a narration different from the mutoon. Finally, he prefers al-Ramli's reconciliation: the mutoon are based on the narration where the grandfather is not obliged (the Quduri view). The teacher should guide students through the layers: the baseline rule in the mutoon, the correction by al-Dhakhirah, the elaboration by Abu Yusuf, and the final reconciliation. The key point is that the Hanafi school has multiple transmitted opinions, and later scholars reconciled them by attributing different rulings to different narrations.

Key Terms
قرابة (qarabah): Relatives, specifically those related by blood (nasab).
محرم (muhrim): A person with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to blood relationship or affinity.
المتون (al-mutoon): The standard texts, i.e., the foundational matn works like Kanz al-Daqaiq which are the basis for the commentary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition do maternal relatives become directly liable for the minor's maintenance (not as a loan)?
A) If they are mahram and heirs.
B) If the father is poor and invalid.
C) If there are no paternal relatives.
D) If the mother is also poor.

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's ruling provides a multi-tiered liability for a minor's maintenance when the father is poor, and the apparent clash with the standard texts is resolved by recognizing different narrations within the Hanafi school.

The Obligation to Support Minor Children When the Father is Poor: An Intra-Hanafi Analysis
Intra-Hanafi dispute on maintenance shift when father is disabled

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on who must support minor children when the father is poor and disabled, and evaluate the conflicting narrations within the school.

Core Idea
The father's poverty alone does not shift maintenance; only disability (zamin) makes him like dead.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about maintenance of minor children. While the matn states categorically that no one shares with the father in maintenance of his child, the reality of a disabled father forces the school to consider exceptions. We will trace the reasoning of Abu Yusuf and the later commentators to understand when the obligation shifts to the grandfather or maternal relatives.

Guided Path
1
State the Default Hanafi Rule
Identify the baseline: maintenance of minor children is wajib on the father alone, and no one shares this obligation.
This is the clear statement in the matn texts.
2
Identify the Exceptional Scenario
The father is not just poor but disabled (zamin), meaning he cannot earn. This triggers a re-evaluation of the rule.
Disability is the key condition that changes the ruling.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's Opinion
According to Abu Yusuf, if the father is zamin, maintenance falls on paternal relatives (including grandfather), and if none, on maternal mahram relatives who are eligible to inherit, as a debt on the father.
This opinion expands the circle of responsibility.
4
Contrast with the Matn's General Statement
The matn says 'no one shares with the father.' This seems to contradict Abu Yusuf. Understand the tension.
The author explains that the obligation on relatives is not 'sharing' but a substitute when the father is like dead.
5
Resolve via Ramli's Tarjih
Ramli prefers the second narration (from al-Quduri) that the grandfather is not obliged unless the father is truly disabled. The matn's statement applies to the normal case.
The relied-upon view: father is not treated as deceased unless zamin; grandfather not obliged in the correct view.

نفقة الأب إن كان وإلا فعلى محارم الصغير من قرابة الأم المستحقين للميراث فإن لم يكن أحد من هؤلاء فهي على أبي الصغير لكن يؤمر قرابة الأم بالإنفاق ديناً على الأب فصار الحاصل أن نفقة الصغير إذا كان أبوه معسراً تجب على الجد الموسر تارة، وتارة على غيره من أقارب الأب وتارة على محارمه من قرابة الأم. فهذا كله يخالف المتون في قولهم لا يشارك الأب في نفقة ولده أحد لكن ذكر في كلام أبي يوسف ما يفيد أن قرابة الأب كالأب أو أن المراد جهته وذلك حيث قال بعد قوله السابق «قضيت بالنفقة على أبيه وأمرت قرابة الأم بالانفاق فيكون ديناً على الأب ما نصه : وهذا لأن قرابة الأم لا يجوز أن يجب عليهم نفقة الولد لما عرف أن الأب لا يشارك غيره في نفقة الصغير، فإذا لم يكن للأب قرابة لم يبق هنا وجه سوى أن يقضي بالنفقة على قرابة الأم ويكون ذلك ديناً على الأب لئلا يشارك الأب غيره في نفقة الولد فأما قرابة الأب مما يلزمهم نفقة الأب فجاز أن . يلزمهم : نفقة الغلام ليكون نفقة ولده جارية مجرى نفقته . هكذا ذكر هذه المسألة في شرح القدوري، وهذا الجواب إنما يستقيم إلى آخر ما ذكره المؤلف، فقد ظهر لك من هذا أن ما وجب على الجد أو غيره من قرابة الأب غير خارج عن قولهم لا يشارك الأب في نفقة ولده بناء على ما قلنا كما هو كالصريح من هذا التعليل. نعم يرد ما أورده صاحب الذخيرة من وجوبها على محارمه من قرابة الأم ولكن بناء على ما عن أبي يوسف فجاز أن يكون ما في المتون جارياً على خلافه. هذا ما ظهر لي والأظهر ما أجاب به الرملي من حمل ما في المتون على الرواية الأخرى يعني ما تقدم عن القدوري من أنها لا تفرض على الجد. وحاصلها أن الأب لا يجعل كالميت مطلقاً إلا إذا كان زمناً لما مر من أنه إذا كان زمناً تجب نفقته على الصغير والكبير الزمن. وفي رواية أن نفقة الكبير تجب على الأبوين أثلاثاً باعتبار الإرث بخلاف الصغير والظاهر الأول .
So too the maintenance of minors. And from Abu Yusuf regarding a minor whose father is needy and disabled: his maintenance is imposed on his paternal relatives without his mother, and everyone who is compelled to support the father is compelled to support the boy. If there is no paternal relative, I would decree maintenance on his father and order the maternal relatives to spend, making it a debt on the father. This answer is only valid if among the maternal relatives there is no one who is a mahram for the minor and eligible for inheritance, because the condition for the obligation of maintenance for non-lineal relatives is mahramiyyah and eligibility for inheritance. If among the maternal relatives there is a mahram for the minor who is eligible for inheritance, then maintenance is obligatory on him, and the poor father is treated like a deceased. The summary: the obligation on the poor father is only if the wealthy mother has spent; otherwise the father is like a deceased and the obligation falls on others as if he were dead, and there is no recourse upon him in the correct view. So this necessitates correcting the matns and commentaries. He said in 'the maintenance of his child' generally... etc.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the maintenance of minor children when the father is poor and disabled (zamin). It presents the view of Abu Yusuf, who distinguishes between cases where the mother is wealthy and where she is not, and whether there are paternal or maternal relatives who are mahram and eligible for inheritance. The author discusses the need to reconcile this with the matn statements that no one shares with the father in maintenance of his child. He analyzes the different scenarios: where the grandfather is wealthy, where the father is disabled, and where maternal mahram relatives exist. The preferred view according to Ramli is to follow the second narration that the grandfather is not obliged, and the father is treated as deceased only if disabled. The passage ends by noting that the maintenance of an adult disabled child is divided between parents in thirds according to one narration.

Key Terms
زمن (zamin): A chronically ill or disabled person who cannot earn a living.
نفقة الصغار (nafaqat al-sighar): Maintenance of minor children, which is obligatory on the father.
الجد (al-jadd): The grandfather (paternal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When he is poor
B) When he is disabled (zamin)
C) When he is absent
D) When the mother is wealthy

Key Takeaway
The father's maintenance obligation for minor children only shifts to relatives when he is disabled (zamin), not merely poor, and the grandfather is obliged only in that scenario.

Wajib Maintenance for Close Mahram Relatives
Explains ruling with proof and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for wajib maintenance of a close mahram relative and explain the Quranic proof.

Core Idea
Maintenance for a close mahram relative is wajib, limited to the heir share, and only if the recipient is poor and unable to earn.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the obligation of nafaqah (maintenance) for close mahram relatives. The author unpacks each condition from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, providing the Quranic proof and illustrative cases. Understanding this ruling is essential for determining financial obligations within the family beyond spouses and children.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Find the phrase that states the obligation: maintenance is wajib for a close mahram relative who is poor and unable to earn, to the extent of that relative's inheritance share.
The ruling is conditional: it only applies when all conditions are met.
2
Extract the conditions
List each condition: (a) the relative must be close (qarib), (b) mahram (marriage permanently prohibited), (c) poor (faqir), (d) unable to earn (ajiz an al-kasb), (e) the amount is limited to the inheritance share (biqadr al-irth).
Note the differentiation: women are assumed unable to earn; men require a valid excuse.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof
The author cites the verse from Al-Baqarah 2:233 and the variant reading of Ibn Mas'ud. Understand how the obligation is derived: the verse imposes maintenance on heirs, and the variant specifies 'mahram', thus limiting it to close mahram relatives who are potential heirs.
In Hanafi usul, the verse is zanni al-dalala, so the ruling is wajib, not fard.
4
Work through the case studies
Examine the examples: maternal uncle vs. cousin, paternal uncle vs. maternal uncle, and the case with paternal uncle, paternal aunt, and maternal aunt. Notice how precedence is determined: first by mahram status, then by actual inheritance entitlement.
If a closer relative is absent, the next in line is compelled to provide maintenance.
5
Understand the gender and special cases
For women, poverty alone triggers the obligation; inability to earn is assumed. For men, there must be a physical or legitimate hindrance (e.g., blindness, chronic illness, or being a student of knowledge). The passage also mentions that a father must support an adult son who cannot earn, even if healthy, if he is a student.
The principle 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' applies: intention matters for students.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولقريب محرم فقير عاجز عن الكسب بقدر الإرث لو موسراً) أي تجب النفقة للقريب إلى آخره لأن الصلة في القرابة القريبة واجبة دون البعيدة، والفاصل أن يكون ذو رحم محرم وقد قال تعالى : ﴿وعلى الوارث مثل ذلك﴾ [البقرة : ٢٣٣] وفي قراءة عبد بن مسعود «وعلى الوارث ذي الرحم المحرم مثل ذلك». قيد بالقريب لأن المحرم الذي ليس بقريب كالأخ من الرضاع لا تجب نفقته . وقيد بالمحرم لأن الرحم غير المحرم لا تجب نفقته كابن العم وإن كان وارثاً ولا بد أن تكون المحرمية بجهة القرابة لأنه لو كان قريباً محرماً لا من جهتها كابن العم إذا كان أخاً من الرضاع فإنه لا نفقة له؛ كذا في شرح الطحاوي، فلو كان له خال وابن عم فالنفقة على الخال لمحرميته لا على ابن العم وإن كان وارثاً لأن المراد من الوارث في الآية من هو أهل للميراث لا كونه وارثاً حقيقة إذ لا يتحقق ذلك إلا بعد الموت والخال وارث في الجملة سواء كان وارثاً في هذه الحالة أو لم يكن . وعند الاستواء في المحرمية وأهلية الإرث يرجح من كان وارثاً حقيقة في هذه الحالة حتى إذا كان له عم وخال فالنفقة على العم لأنهما استويا في المحرمية، ويترجح العم على الخال لكونه وارثاً حقيقة، وكذلك إذا كان له عم وعمة وخالة فالنفقة على العم لا غير إن كان موسراً، وإن كان معسراً فالنفقة على العمة والخالة أثلاثاً على قدر ميراثهما ويجعل العم كالميت. وفي القنية : يجبر الأبعد إذا غاب الأقرب وقيد بالفقر لأن الغني نفقته على نفسه. وقيد بالعجز عن الكسب وهو بالأنوثة مطلقاً وبالزمانة والعمى ونحوها في الذكر، فنفقة المرأة الصحيحة الفقيرة على محرمها فلا يعتبر في الأنثى إلا الفقر وأما البالغ الفقير فلا بد من عجزه بزمانة أو فقء العينين أو شلل اليدين أو مقطوع الرجلين أو معتوه أو مفلوج. زاد في التبيين أن يكون من أعيان الناس يلحقه العار من التكسب أو طالب علم لا يتفرغ لذلك . وفي المجتبى : البالغ إذا كان عاجزاً عن الكسب وهو صحيح فنفقته على الأب، وهكذا قالوا في طالب العلم إذا كان لا يهتدي إلى الكسب لا تسقط نفقته عن الأب بمنزلة الزمن والأنثى اهـ.
He said: (And for a close mahram relative who is poor, unable to earn, to the extent of inheritance were he wealthy) i.e. maintenance is obligatory for the relative etc., because the connection in close kinship is obligatory, not distant. The distinguishing factor is being a relative of mahram. And Allah Most High said: 'And upon the heir is the like of that.' [Al-Baqarah 2:233] And in the reading of Abd ibn Mas'ud: 'And upon the heir who is a mahram relative is the like of that.' He restricted it to 'close' because a mahram who is not a close relative, like a foster brother, his maintenance is not obligatory. And he restricted it to 'mahram' because a non-mahram relative, like a cousin, even if he is an heir, his maintenance is not obligatory. And it is necessary that the mahram status be through lineage, because if he is a close mahram not through that, like a cousin if he is a foster brother, then there is no maintenance for him; as in Sharh al-Tahawi. So if someone has a maternal uncle and a cousin, maintenance is on the maternal uncle due to his mahram status, not on the cousin even if he is an heir, because the intended meaning of 'heir' in the verse is one who is eligible for inheritance, not that he is an actual heir, since that is only realized after death, and the maternal uncle is a heir in a general sense whether he is an actual heir in this situation or not. When they are equal in mahram status and eligibility for inheritance, precedence is given to the one who is an actual heir in that situation. Thus, if someone has a paternal uncle and a maternal uncle, maintenance is on the paternal uncle because they are equal in mahram status, and the paternal uncle is preferred over the maternal uncle because he is an actual heir. Similarly, if he has a paternal uncle, a paternal aunt, and a maternal aunt, maintenance is on the paternal uncle alone if he is wealthy; if he is poor, then maintenance is on the paternal aunt and maternal aunt in thirds according to their inheritance shares, and the paternal uncle is treated as dead. In al-Qunyah: The more distant is compelled if the closer is absent. And he restricted it to poverty because the wealthy maintain themselves. And he restricted it to inability to earn, which for females is absolute, and for males is by chronic illness, blindness, and the like. So the maintenance of a healthy poor woman is on her mahram; thus for females only poverty is considered. As for a poor male, it is conditional on his inability due to chronic illness, loss of eyes, paralysis of hands, amputation of legs, insanity, or paralysis. In al-Tabyin, it is added that he should be from the nobles whom earning would disgrace, or a student of knowledge who cannot spare time for that. In al-Mujtaba: An adult who is unable to earn while healthy, his maintenance is on the father. Similarly they said about a student of knowledge: if he cannot find a way to earn, his maintenance is not dropped from the father, like the chronically ill and the female.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn phrase regarding maintenance for a close mahram relative. The author explains that maintenance is wajib on a wealthy person for his close mahram relatives who are poor and unable to earn, to the extent of the relative's inheritance share. The proof is the Qur'anic verse and its variant reading. The author then breaks down each condition: (1) 'close' excludes non-close relations like foster brothers; (2) 'mahram' excludes non-mahram relatives like cousins even if they are heirs; (3) 'poor' means the recipient must be in need; (4) 'unable to earn' means for women it is absolute (they are not expected to work), for men it requires a valid excuse like chronic illness, disability, or being a student who cannot work. The passage also discusses cases where multiple relatives are eligible: precedence is given based on mahram status and actual inheritance entitlement. For example, a maternal uncle is preferred over a cousin, but a paternal uncle is preferred over a maternal uncle when both are present. If a paternal uncle is poor, maintenance falls on paternal and maternal aunts proportionally. The text also notes that a student of knowledge who is unable to earn is treated like a disabled person, and his maintenance is wajib on his father.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to blood or fosterage.
وارث (warith): A person who is eligible to inherit from the relative; does not require actual inheritance at the time.
عجز عن الكسب (ajz an al-kasb): Inability to earn a livelihood due to disability, chronic illness, or other valid reasons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the share of maintenance for a close mahram relative?
A) Equal to the amount of zakat
B) To the extent of the relative's inheritance share from the maintainer
C) Up to the maintainer's ability
D) A fixed amount determined by the qadi

Key Takeaway
Maintenance for a close mahram relative is wajib under four conditions: the relative must be close, mahram, poor, and unable to earn, and the amount is limited to the inheritance share.

Maintenance for Students of Knowledge
Intra-Hanafi dispute on maintenance for students

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on the obligation of a father to support a student of knowledge and evaluate the author's tarjih.

Core Idea
Maintenance for a student of knowledge is wajib if sincere, not if corrupt.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses an intra-Hanafi debate: does a father have a wajib obligation to support his adult son who is a student of religious knowledge? The salaf held it wajib, but later scholar Abu Hamid issued a fatwa against it due to the corruption of most students. The author resolves the dispute by distinguishing sincere students from frivolous ones, and his preferred view provides the operative ruling for fatwa today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Hanafi positions
State the two views: (1) wajib based on salaf, (2) not wajib due to student corruption. Note the opposing scholar (Abu Hamid).
2
Analyze the basis for the second position
Examine why Abu Hamid opposed the salaf: he saw most students as corrupt, engaging in useless arguments and backbiting, thus undeserving.
This is a change based on reality (taghayyur al-ahkam bi taghayyur al-zaman).
3
Follow the author's response
The author argues that sincere students of beneficial sciences (fiqh, adab) are entitled, and corrupt ones are exceptional. He mentions a later time when earning prevents study, so the salaf's view is chosen.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The author's preferred view: obligation for sincere students. The fatwa must distinguish based on the student's conduct and field of study.
This is the operative ruling for contemporary fatwa.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: a father can be compelled to support a genuine student, but not a frivolous one. Fatwa depends on case-by-case assessment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : والظاهر أنه لم يخف على أبي حامد قول السلف بوجوب نفقة طالب العلم على الأب لكن أفتى بعدم وجوبها لفساد أحوال أكثر طلبة العلم فإن من كان منهم مشتغلاً بالعلوم النافعة يجبر الآباء على الإنفاق عليهم وإنما يطالبهم فساق المبتدعة الذين شرهم أكثر من خيرهم يحضرون الدرس ساعة بخلافيات ركيكة ضررها في الدين أكثر من نفعها ثم يشتغلون طول النهار بالسخرية والغيبة والوقوع في الناس مما يستحقون به لعنة الله والملائكة والناس أجمعين، فيقذف الله البغض في قلوب آبائهم وينزع عنهم الشفقة فلا يعطون مناهم في الملابس والمطاعم فيطالبونهم بالنفقة ويؤذونهم مع حرمة التأفيف، ولو علموا بسيرتهم السلف الحرموا الإنفاق عليهم، ومن كان بخلافهم نادر في هذا الزمان فلا يفرد بالحكم دفعاً لخرج التمييز بين المصلح والمفسد . قلت لكن نرى طلبة العلم بعد الفتنة العامة المشتغلين بالفقه والأدب اللذين هما قواعد الدين وأصول كلام العرب والاشتغال بالكسب يمنعهم عن التحصيل ويؤدي إلى ضياع العلم والتعطيل فكان المختار الآن قول السلف، وهفوات البعض لا تمنع وجوب النفقة كالأولاد والأقارب اهـ.
And in al-Qunyah: It appears that it was not hidden from Abu Hamid the statement of the predecessors (salaf) about the obligation of maintenance of a student of knowledge on the father, but he gave a fatwa against its obligation due to the corruption of the conditions of most students of knowledge. For those among them who are engaged in beneficial sciences, fathers are compelled to spend on them. Rather, it is the sinful innovators who demand it, whose evil is more than their good. They attend the lesson for an hour with flimsy disputations harmful to religion. Then they spend the whole day mocking, backbiting, and attacking people, deserving the curse of Allah, the angels, and all people. So Allah casts hatred into the hearts of their fathers and removes compassion from them, so they do not fulfill their desires in clothing and food, and [the students] demand maintenance from them and harm them despite the prohibition of saying 'uff'. If the predecessors knew their conduct, they would forbid spending on them. And whoever is contrary to them is rare in this era, so the ruling is not singled out for them to avoid the difficulty of distinguishing between the reformer and the corrupt. I say: But we see the students of knowledge after the general fitna, those engaged in fiqh and adab, which are the foundations of the religion and the principles of Arabic speech, and engagement in earning prevents them from study and leads to the loss and neglect of knowledge. So the chosen view now is the statement of the predecessors, and the lapses of some do not prevent the obligation of maintenance like for children and relatives.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a father must provide maintenance for his adult son who is a student of religious knowledge. The predecessors (salaf) held it obligatory, but later scholar Abu Hamid (possibly Abu Hamid al-Ghazali or another) gave a fatwa against it due to the corrupt state of many students who misuse their time. However, the author of al-Qunyah notes that if the student is sincerely engaged in beneficial sciences, the father should be compelled. The author then gives his own opinion ('قلت'): after the general fitna, students of fiqh and Arabic literature are deserving of support because earning would prevent their studies. Thus the preferred view is the original one of obligation. The passage also warns against frivolous students who waste time in argumentation and backbiting.

Key Terms
طالب العلم (talib al-ilm): A person engaged in seeking religious knowledge, typically formal studies of fiqh, hadith, etc.
السلف (al-salaf): The early generations of Muslims, including the Companions and successors, whose practice is a source of authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred view, who among the following would be entitled to maintenance from his father?
A) A student who spends his time in useless disputations and backbiting
B) A student who is sincerely studying fiqh and Arabic literature but cannot work
C) Any student regardless of conduct
D) Only a student of hadith, not fiqh

Key Takeaway
Maintenance for a student of knowledge is wajib on his father only if the student is sincere and engaged in beneficial religious sciences.

Definition of Indigent (Mu'sir) for Nafaqah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on indigent definition for nafaqah entitlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi riwayat on whether ownership of a house and servant affects nafaqah entitlement, identify the mufta bihi, and explain the legal reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Owning a house and servant does not disqualify one from nafaqah — need is not negated by basic assets.

The Hanafi definition of 'mu'sir' (indigent) for nafaqah is not simply being destitute. This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does ownership of a house and a servant negate need? We explore the two transmitted opinions and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Define the term mu'sir
Identify the two general definitions given in the passage: eligible for sadaqah vs. simply needy.
These are broader definitions; the specific dispute focuses on a person with a house and servant.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Focus on the scenario: a person who owns a house and servant. Is he entitled to nafaqah from a wealthy relative?
There is ikhtilaf al-riwayah — two narrations within the school.
3
Analyze the two riwayat
First riwaya: no entitlement, even for a sister or daughter. Second riwaya: entitlement exists. Note the underlying ta'lil (qiyas vs. istihsan).
The first view analogizes to liquid assets; the second considers selling essential assets as undue hardship.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Bada'i declares the second riwaya correct (al-sawab). This is the operative position for fatwa.
The tarjih is based on the objective of nafaqah and istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في حد المعسر الذي يستحق هذه النفقة فقيل هو الذي تحل له هذه الصدقة، وقيل هو المحتاج. والذي له منزل وخادم هل يستحق النفقة على قريبه الموسر؟ فيه اختلاف الرواية، في رواية لا يستحق حتى لو كانت أختاً لا يؤمر الأخ بالإنفاق عليها، وكذا لو كانت بنتاً وأما في رواية تستحق وهو الصواب، كذا في البدائع، وأطلق المصنف فيمن تجب عليه هذه النفقة فشمل الصغير الغني والصغيرة الغنية فيؤمر الوصي بدفع نفقة قريبهما المحرم بشرطه ؛ كذا في أنفع الوسائل أيضاً وقدمناه، وأفاد بقوله بقدر الميراث» أنه لو تعدد من تجب عليه النفقة فإنها تقسم عليهم بقدر ميراثهم لأن الله أوجب النفقة باسم الوارث
And they differed regarding the definition of the indigent who is entitled to this maintenance. It was said: he is one for whom sadaqah (charity) is permissible. And it was said: he is the needy person. And one who has a house and a servant, is he entitled to maintenance from his wealthy relative? There is a difference of riwaya. In one riwaya he is not entitled, even if it is a sister, the brother is not commanded to spend on her; similarly if it is a daughter. In another riwaya he is entitled, and that is correct. Such is in al-Bada'i. And the author (of the matn) spoke generally regarding whom this maintenance is obligatory, so it includes the wealthy minor boy and the wealthy minor girl, so the guardian is ordered to pay maintenance for their mahram relative with its condition; as in Anfa' al-Wasa'il also, and we have presented it. And his statement 'according to the inheritance share' indicates that if there are multiple persons upon whom maintenance is obligatory, it is divided among them according to their inheritance shares, because Allah obligated maintenance in the name of the heir.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: (1) The definition of 'mu'sir' (indigent) who qualifies for nafaqah from relatives. There are two opinions: one says it is the person eligible for zakat/sadaqah (i.e., under the nisab), the other says it is simply the needy person (who lacks basic necessities). (2) A specific scenario: if a person owns a house and a servant (i.e., basic shelter and help), does he still deserve nafaqah? There are two narrations (riwayat) in the school: one says no, because he can sell the house and servant to meet needs; the other says yes, because selling such assets is rare and not required. The author of al-Bada'i favors the latter (entitled). The passage also clarifies that the matn's phrase 'بقدر الإرث' applies when multiple relatives are liable: their shares are divided proportionally according to their inheritance portions.

Key Terms
معسر (mu'sir): A person who is in financial difficulty and unable to meet basic needs.
رواية (riwayah): A transmission of a legal opinion within the Hanafi school, often from different disciples.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view (al-sawab) in the Hanafi school, is a person who owns a house and servant entitled to maintenance from a wealthy relative?
A) No, because he can sell them
B) Yes, because selling such assets is rare
C) Only if he is a student of knowledge
D) Only if he has no other relatives

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi is that ownership of basic necessities like a house and servant does not make one ineligible for nafaqah from a wealthy relative.
