When Is Maintenance Obligatory for a Student?
Reconciling the obligation of fathers to spend on students with the corruption of some students

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father is obligated to provide maintenance for his student son, and identify when the obligation ceases due to corruption.

Core Idea
Maintenance of a student is obligatory if he is sincere and studies beneficial sciences; corrupt students forfeit this right.

This passage navigates a real-life tension: fathers are generally obliged to support their sons who are students, but some students abuse the system. The author, Ibn Abidin, discusses a fatwa from Hasan ibn Ziyad that initially obliged maintenance, then a later fatwa exempting corrupt students, and finally gives his own preferred view that sincere students remain entitled. The key is distinguishing beneficial study from destructive behavior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default Hanafi ruling
Start with the general principle: a father is obligated to provide maintenance (nafaqah) for his children who are unable to earn a living, including those engaged in study. This is the default.
Based on the Qur'an and sunnah, support of children is obligatory.
2
Recognize the exception for corrupt students
The author narrates a fatwa that maintenance is not obligatory for students who are corrupt innovators (fussāq al-mubtadi‘ah), because their activity is harmful and they waste time in backbiting and mockery.
The exception is based on the purpose of maintenance: to enable beneficial study, not to fund destructive behavior.
3
Analyze the criteria: beneficial vs harmful
Beneficial sciences (al-‘ulūm al-nāfi‘ah) include fiqh and adab, which are foundations of religion. Harmful activity includes engaging in weak disagreements (khilāfiyyāt rakīkah) and wasting time in sin.
The author uses the phrase 'whose harm is greater than their good'.
4
Follow the author's chosen position
The author sides with the earlier (Salaf) view: maintenance is obligatory for sincere students of fiqh and adab, even if some students are corrupt. The lapses of a few do not negate the general obligation.
He says 'the chosen view now is the saying of the Salaf'.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Consider a son who attends a madrasa but spends most of his time on social media and backbiting. According to the fatwa, his father is not obligated to fund his lifestyle. Conversely, a son diligently studying fiqh and Arabic deserves support.
The evaluation depends on the actual conduct, not just the label 'student'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهم حسن على الأب لكن أفتى بعدم وجوبها لفساد أحوال أكثر طلبة العلم فإن من كان مشتغلاً بالعلوم النافعة يجبر الآباء على الإنفاق عليهم وإنما يطالبهم فساق المبتدعة السيرة الذين شرهم أكثر من خيرهم يحضرون الدرس ساعة بخلافيات ركيكة ضررها في الدين أكثر من نفعها ثم يشتغلون طول النهار بالسخرية والغيبة والوقوع في الناس مما يستحقون به لعنة الله والملائكة والناس أجمعين، فيقذف الله البغض في قلوب آبائهم وينزغ عنهم الشفقة فلا يعطون مناهم في الملابس والمطاعم فيطالبونهم بالنفقة ويؤذونهم مع حرمة التأفيف، ولو علموا بسيرتهم السلف الحرموا الإنفاق عليهم، ومن كان بخلافهم نادر في هذا الزمان فلا يفرد بالحكم دفعاً لخرج التمييز بين المصلح والمفسد . قلت لكن نرى طلبة العلم بعد الفتنة العامة المشتغلين بالفقه والأدب اللذين هما قواعد الدين وأصول كلام العرب والاشتغال بالكسب يمنعهم عن التحصيل ويؤدي إلى ضياع العلم والتعطيل فكان المختار الآن قول السلف، وهفوات البعض لا تمنع وجوب النفقة كالأولاد والأقارب اهـ.
Among them is Hasan: it is obligatory upon the father, but he gave a fatwa that it is not obligatory due to the corruption of the conditions of most students of knowledge. For whoever is engaged in beneficial sciences, fathers are compelled to spend on them. They are only demanded by the corrupt innovators of bad conduct, whose evil is greater than their good. They attend the lesson for an hour with weak disagreements, whose harm in religion is greater than their benefit, then they spend the rest of the day in mockery, backbiting, and slandering people, thereby deserving the curse of Allah, the angels, and all mankind. So Allah casts hatred into the hearts of their fathers and removes compassion from them, so they do not give them their desires in clothing and food, and they demand maintenance from them and harm them despite the prohibition of saying 'uff'. If the Salaf knew their conduct, they would forbid spending on them. And whoever is contrary to them is rare in this time, so the ruling is not singled out for them to avoid the difficulty of distinguishing between the righteous and the corrupt. I say: but we see students of knowledge after the general fitna, those engaged in fiqh and adab, which are the foundations of the religion and the principles of the speech of the Arabs, and engaging in earning prevents them from learning and leads to the loss of knowledge and disruption. So the chosen view now is the saying of the Salaf, and the lapses of some do not prevent the obligation of maintenance like for children and relatives. End.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a fatwa-style ruling discussing the maintenance obligation of fathers for their sons who are students of knowledge. The author first narrates from Hasan (likely Hasan ibn Ziyad) that maintenance is obligatory on the father, but later scholars gave a fatwa against its obligation due to the corrupt state of most students. The author then distinguishes between sincere students of beneficial sciences, who do deserve maintenance, and corrupt students who waste time in harmful activities. The author notes that today, after the general fitna, the chosen position is that of the Salaf: fathers are obliged to support their children who are sincerely studying fiqh and adab, as these are the foundations of the religion. The lapses of a few do not negate the general obligation, just as for children and relatives.

Key Terms
طلبة العلم (talabat al-‘ilm): Students of Islamic knowledge, here specifically those studying in a madrasa setting.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and shelter, obligatory on relatives within specified degrees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The son is over 18 years old.
D) The father is poor.

Key Takeaway
A father's obligation to maintain his student son is conditional on the son's sincere pursuit of beneficial knowledge; corruption and harmful conduct can remove that obligation.

Definition of Insolvency and the House & Servant Dispute for Maintenance
Intra-Hanafi dispute on insolvency and effect of owning assets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi definitions of mu'sir and explain the two narrations regarding a person who owns a house and servant, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Insolvency is defined by either zakat eligibility or mere need; owning a house and servant does not automatically disqualify one from relative maintenance.

Maintenance (nafaqah) is wajib on a wealthy relative for a needy relative, but who exactly is 'needy'? The Hanafi school records two definitions of insolvency (mu'sir). A further dispute arises when the potential recipient owns a house and a servant. Are they still entitled? The passage presents two narrations from Abu Hanifa, with later authorities preferring one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of mu'sir
From the opening of the passage, note the two positions: (1) the one for whom charity (sadaqah) is permissible (i.e., the faqir who qualifies for zakat), and (2) the one who is simply needy (muhtaj).
These definitions affect who is entitled to maintenance from relatives.
2
Analyze the dispute on the house and servant owner
The passage presents two narrations: one disallows maintenance for anyone owning a house and servant; the other allows it. Understand the reasoning behind each: first narration says they are not needy because they can sell, second says selling is rare and not everyone can rent.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Determine the mufta bihi position
The commentary cites al-Ramli, al-Mahbubi, al-Nasafi, and Sadr al-Shari'ah that the first narration (not entitled) is the apparent one and basis for fatwa. However, the author of al-Bada'i' considers the second narration correct.
The later preferred view is that such a person is not entitled, but the matter has practical nuance.
4
Understand the distribution of maintenance
The passage also explains that if multiple relatives are obligated, maintenance is divided according to their inheritance shares, because the obligation is by virtue of being an heir.
This applies to the overall framework of nafaqah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في حد المعسر الذي يستحق هذه النفقة فقيل هو الذي تحل له هذه الصدقة، وقيل هو المحتاج. والذي له منزل وخادم هل يستحق النفقة على قريبه الموسر؟ فيه اختلاف الرواية، في رواية لا يستحق حتى لو كانت أختاً لا يؤمر الأخ بالإنفاق عليها، وكذا لو كانت بنتاً وأما في رواية تستحق وهو الصواب، كذا في البدائع، وأطلق المصنف فيمن تجب عليه هذه النفقة فشمل الصغير الغني والصغيرة الغنية فيؤمر الوصي بدفع نفقة قريبهما المحرم بشرطه ؛ كذا في أنفع الوسائل أيضاً وقدمناه، وأفاد بقوله بقدر الميراث» أنه لو تعدد من تجب عليه النفقة فإنها تقسم عليهم بقدر ميراثهم لأن الله أوجب النفقة باسم الوارث

قوله : (والظاهر الأول أي ظاهر الرواية كما نقله الرملي عن الشيخ قاسم قال وقال المحبوبي : وبه يفتى. ومشى عليه النسفي وصدر الشريعة قوله : (وهو) بالأنوثة مطلقاً) أي بلا قيد زمانة أو عمى، ومثل الأنوثة الصغر وقد مر عند قول المتن ولطفله الفقير إن الأب الغني تجب عليه نفقة ابنه الصغير الفقير إلى أن يبلغ حد الكسب وإن لم يبلغ الحلم فهنا بالأولى حتى لو كان له كسب يكفيه لا تجب نفقته على القريب، وكذا الأنثى على ما قدمناه عن حاشية الرملي قوله : (والذي له منزل وخادم الخ) قال في الذخيرة لو كان للأب مسكن أو دابة فالمذهب عندنا أن تفرض النفقة على الابن إلا أن يكون في المسكن فضل نحو أن يكفيه أن يسكن ناحية منه فيؤمر الأب ببيع الفضل والإنفاق على نفسه ثم تفرض نفقته على ابنه ، وكذا إذا كان له دابة نفيسة يؤمر أن يبيعها ويشتري الأوكس وينفق على الابن ويستوي في هذا الوالدان والمولودون وسائر المحارم وهو الصحيح من المذهب لكن قال في البدائع بعدما نقله المؤلف عنها وجه الرواية الأولى أن النفقة لا تجب لغير المحتاج وهؤلاء غير محتاجين لأنه يمكن الاكتفاء بالأدنى بأن يبيع المنزل كله أو بعضه ويكتري منزلاً أو يبيع الخادم، ووجه الرواية الثانية أن بيع المنزل لا يقع إلا نادراً وكذا لا يمكن كل أحد السكني بالكراء
They differed concerning the definition of the insolvent who is entitled to this maintenance: it is said that it is the one for whom charity is permissible, and it is said that it is the needy. And the one who has a house and a servant: is he entitled to maintenance from his wealthy relative? There is difference of narration. In one narration, he is not entitled, even if it is a sister, the brother is not ordered to spend on her, and likewise if it is a daughter. But in another narration, he is entitled, and that is correct, as in al-Bada'i. And the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) gave an absolute statement regarding those on whom this maintenance is obligatory, so it includes the wealthy minor boy and the wealthy minor girl, so the executor is ordered to pay the maintenance of their relatives within the prohibited degrees under its condition, as in Anfa' al-Wasa'il and we have mentioned it. And he indicated by his statement 'according to the inheritance' that if there are multiple people on whom maintenance is obligatory, it is divided among them according to their inheritance, because Allah made maintenance obligatory by virtue of inheritance.

He said: (and the apparent is the first) i.e., the apparent narration, as al-Ramli transmitted from Shaykh Qasim who said, and al-Mahbubi said: and by it fatwa is given. Al-Nasafi and Sadr al-Shari'ah relied on it. He said: (and it is) with femininity absolutely, i.e., without restriction of chronic illness or blindness, and like femininity is minority. And it has passed in the statement of the text 'and for his poor child' that the wealthy father is obliged to provide maintenance for his poor minor son until he reaches the age of earning even if he has not reached puberty, so here it is even more so, so that even if he has earnings sufficient for him, his maintenance is not obligatory on the relative, and likewise for the female as we have mentioned from the marginalia of al-Ramli. He said: (and the one who has a house and servant, etc.) al-Dhakhirah said: If the father has a dwelling or an animal, the madhhab with us is that maintenance is imposed on the son, unless there is surplus in the dwelling such that it suffices him to live in one part of it, then the father is ordered to sell the surplus and spend on himself, then his maintenance is imposed on his son. Likewise, if he has a valuable animal, he is ordered to sell it and buy a cheaper one and spend on the son. This is equal for parents, children, and all other mahram relatives, and it is the correct view of the madhhab. But al-Bada'i' said after the author transmitted from it: the basis of the first narration is that maintenance is not obligatory for other than the needy, and these are not needy because it is possible to suffice with the lesser by selling the whole house or part of it and renting a house, or selling the servant. And the basis of the second narration is that selling a house rarely occurs, and likewise not everyone can live in a rented house.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the definition of 'insolvent' (mu'sir) for entitlement to maintenance from wealthy relatives. Two definitions are given: it is the one for whom charity is permissible (i.e., the poor person who qualifies for zakat), or the one who is simply needy (muhtaj). The author then discusses a more specific issue: if a person owns a house and a servant, are they still entitled to maintenance from a wealthy relative? There are two narrations within the Hanafi school: one says no, they are not entitled, and the other says yes, they are entitled. The author mentions that the correct view is that they are entitled, as in al-Bada'i. However, the commentary then cites al-Dhakhirah which presents the preferred view: the father is ordered to sell any surplus property and only then is maintenance imposed on the son. The basis of the differing narrations is whether selling the house is considered feasible or not. The preferred view according to the commentary is that a person with a house and servant is still entitled because selling a house is not typical. The author also notes that the maintenance is divided among multiple obligated relatives according to their shares of inheritance, as the obligation is by virtue of being an heir.

Key Terms
معسر (mu‘sir): The insolvent person who cannot fulfill his own basic needs and is entitled to maintenance from relatives.
اختلاف الرواية (ikhtilaf al-riwayah): A difference of transmitted rulings within the Hanafi school, often attributed to different students of Abu Hanifa.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and housing, obligatory on certain relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), is a person who owns a house and servant entitled to maintenance from their wealthy relative?
A) Yes, absolutely.
B) No, because they are not considered needy.
C) Only if they are female.
D) Only if the relative is a parent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two definitions of insolvency and a dispute on assets; the preferred view is that a person with a house and servant is not entitled to maintenance, but the reasoning relies on the feasibility of selling surplus property.

Division of Maintenance According to Inheritance Shares
Explaining the division of maintenance according to inheritance shares and the legal fiction of treating the insolvent as non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that maintenance is divided according to inheritance shares and apply the legal fiction of treating an insolvent relative as non-existent to determine liability.

Core Idea
Maintenance follows inheritance: each relative's share of upkeep is proportional to their share of the estate.

This passage builds on the rule that maintenance (nafaqah) of a child is the responsibility of those who would inherit from the child. It explores several scenarios involving mothers, uncles, brothers, and sisters, and introduces a key legal fiction: when a close relative is insolvent, they are treated as non-existent, shifting the duty to the next in line. Understanding this principle is essential for correctly assigning maintenance obligations in complex family situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State that maintenance is divided among those who inherit from the child, according to their inheritance shares.
This is the core principle derived from the text.
2
Apply the rule to simple cases
Work through examples: mother and uncle, mother and brother. See that breastfeeding costs are also divided in thirds as a form of maintenance.
Breastfeeding is analogized (qiyas) to post-weaning maintenance.
3
Recognize the legal fiction (yuj‘al ka al-ma‘dum)
When a closer relative is insolvent, they are treated as non-existent, allowing liability to shift to more distant relatives who would inherit in their absence.
This prevents unfair burden on the insolvent party.
4
Apply to the case of a poor, chronically ill father
The father is poor and chronically ill; his young insolvent son is treated as non-existent. The father's maintenance falls on the son's brothers (the father's brothers) in sixths.
The father must be chronically ill (zaman) to be considered non-existent; mere poverty is insufficient.
5
Distinguish cases involving daughters vs. sons
If the child is a daughter, she inherits with the father, so the brother from father and mother is liable alone. With a son, sisters do not inherit, so they share in fifths after treating the son as non-existent.
This shows how inheritance rules directly determine maintenance division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بنت فنفقة الأب على الأخ لأب وأم خاصة لأنا لا نحتاج أن نجعلها كالمعدوم لأنه يرث مع البنت وقد تعذر إيجاب النفقة على البنت فيجب على الأخ لأب وأم ونفقة الصغير على العم والأخ خاصة لأن الأب المعسر كالمعدوم، وبعد الأب ميراث الولد للعم للأب والأم خاصة فكذا نفقة الولد عليهما . فإن كان مكان الإخوة أخوات متفرقات، فإن كان الولد ذكراً فنفقة الأب على الأخوات أخماساً، لأن أحداً من الأخوات لا يرث مع الابن فلا بد أن يجعل الابن كالمعدوم ليمكن إيجاب النفقة على الأخوات وبعد الابن ميراث الأب وبالمنزل المشترك وهذا هو الصواب ا هـ قوله : (فيه اختلاف الرواية أقول : والظاهر أن المتاع بمنزلة المنزل والخادم في جريان الخلاف المذكور فيه وفي التتارخانية عن العيون: ولو أن امرأة لها منزل وخادم ومتاع ولا فضل في شيء من ذلك ولها أخ موسر أو عم موسر وطلبت النفقة فإن القاضي يجبره عليها : هكذا قال الخصاف. وقال غيره : لا يجبر ويقال لها بيعي دارك وخادمك. وقال يحيى بن آدم : الأمر عندنا أنه لا يجبر على نفقتها إذا كان لها خادم ومتاع ا هـ . قوله: (وأما ما يحتاج النفقة قبل الفطام والرضاع كله على الأم قال الرملي : الظاهر أن الجواب في الحضانة كذلك فيجري فيها ما يجري في الرضاع فيكون ظاهر الرواية أجرة الحضانة أيضاً على الأم والأخ أثلاثاً بحسب الميراث لاحتياجه إليها كاحتياجه إلى النفقة وقد كتبناه في باب الحضانة قوله : (وإذا كان للفقير الزمن الخ) قيد بالزمن لأن الأب إذا كان فقيراً غير زمن لا يجعل كالميت على ما تقدم من أن الأم الموسرة تنفق على الصغار لترجع على الأب، وكذا الجد بناء ما مر عن القدوري والحسن بن صالح من أن النفقة لا تجب على الجد وإنما يؤمر بها ديناً على الأب، وقد علمت مما مر أن أصحاب المتون والشروح اختاروا هذه الرواية على خلاف ما صححه في الذخيرة قوله : (وليس بزمن) الذي إليه من بين الأخوات
So it is necessary to estimate by it. So if a minor has a mother and an uncle, or a mother and a brother from the father and mother, the maintenance on both of them is according to the inheritance. Likewise, breastfeeding is on both of them in thirds, because breastfeeding is maintenance of the child, so it is on both of them like his maintenance after weaning. Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that the maintenance after weaning is the answer like this. As for what is needed before weaning, all breastfeeding is on the mother because she is wealthy by milk, and the uncle is insolvent in that, but in the apparent narration, the uncle's ability to obtain that with his wealth makes him wealthy regarding it, so for this reason it is between them in thirds. If the uncle is poor and the mother is rich, all is on the mother. And if he has a mother and a brother from the mother and father or a brother and an uncle who are wealthy, then breastfeeding is on the mother and the brother in thirds according to the inheritance, because the uncle is not a heir in this case, so the brother takes precedence over the uncle. And if the poor, chronically ill father has a young son who is insolvent and not chronically ill, and this insolvent son has three brothers, different, all wealthy, then the man's maintenance is on the brother from the father and mother and the brother from the mother in sixths, because the young insolvent son is considered non-existent regarding the obligation of maintenance on others. As long as the son is not considered non-existent, the brothers do not become heirs, so it becomes impossible to oblige maintenance on them while the son exists. So the son is considered non-existent, and the inheritance is made between the brother from the father and mother and the brother from the mother in sixths. And if instead of a son there is a daughter, then the father's maintenance is on the brother from the father and mother only, because we have no need to consider her non-existent because he inherits with the daughter, and since maintenance cannot be obliged on the daughter, it is obliged on the brother from the father and mother. And the maintenance of the young child is on the uncle and the brother specifically, because the insolvent father is considered non-existent, and after the father, the child's inheritance is for the uncle from the father and mother specifically, so likewise the child's maintenance is on them both. And if instead of brothers there are different sisters, then if the child is male, the father's maintenance is on the sisters in fifths, because none of the sisters inherit with the son, so it is necessary to consider the son non-existent to make it possible to oblige maintenance on the sisters, and after the son the father's inheritance is in the common residence, and this is the correct. End. He said: (there is difference of narration in it) I say: and the apparent is that furnishings are like the house and servant in the running of the aforementioned dispute in it. And in al-Tatar Khaniyya from al-Uyun: If a woman has a house, servant, and furnishings, and there is no surplus in any of that, and she has a wealthy brother or a wealthy uncle, and she asks for maintenance, then the judge compels him for it. This is what al-Khasaf said. Others said: he is not compelled, and it is said to her: sell your house and your servant. And Yahya ibn Adam said: the matter with us is that he is not compelled for her maintenance if she has a servant and furnishings. End. He said: (and as for what is needed for maintenance before weaning, all breastfeeding is on the mother). Al-Ramli said: the apparent is that the answer in custody is likewise, so what applies in breastfeeding applies in it. So the apparent narration is that the custody fee is also on the mother and the brother in thirds according to inheritance, because he needs it like he needs maintenance, and we have written it in the chapter on custody. He said: (and if for the poor, chronically ill, etc.) He restricted it with 'chronically ill' because if the father is poor but not chronically ill, he is not considered dead, as previously mentioned that the wealthy mother spends on the young children to take recourse against the father, and likewise the grandfather based on what passed from al-Quduri and al-Hasan ibn Salih that maintenance is not obligatory on the grandfather, rather he is ordered to pay it as a debt on the father. And you have already learned from what passed that the authors of the texts and commentaries chose this narration contrary to what was corrected in al-Dhakhirah. He said: (and he is not chronically ill) which is from among the sisters.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is an extensive expansion on the principle that maintenance among multiple obligated relatives is divided according to their shares of inheritance. The author provides several examples to illustrate the application. First, for a minor child with a mother and uncle (or mother and brother), maintenance and breastfeeding costs are divided in proportion to their inheritance shares. Breastfeeding is treated like maintenance because it is a form of upkeep. The author then discusses the principle of treating an insolvent relative as non-existent (ma'dum) for the purpose of shifting maintenance to more distant relatives. This is illustrated with the case of a poor, chronically ill father, his young insolvent son, and three wealthy brothers. The insolvent son is treated as non-existent, so the brothers inherit from the father and are liable for his maintenance in sixths. If the child is a daughter, the brother from father and mother is responsible because the daughter inherits. The passage also includes the commentary that furnishings are analogous to house and servant in the dispute about entitlement, and that custody fees follow the same rule as breastfeeding. Finally, the author notes that the father must be chronically ill to be considered non-existent; otherwise, maintenance is a debt on him.

Key Terms
يجعل كالمعدوم (yuj‘al ka al-ma‘dum): A legal fiction where a relative is considered non-existent for the purpose of allocating maintenance to other relatives.
زمن (zaman): Chronically ill or disabled, a condition that prevents earning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Equally among the three brothers.
B) According to the inheritance shares of the son's brothers: the brother from the father and mother and the brother from the mother receive sixths.
C) Only on the brother from the father and mother.
D) It is not obligatory because the son exists.

Key Takeaway
Maintenance obligations among relatives mirror inheritance shares, with the legal fiction 'yuj‘al ka al-ma‘dum' shifting liability when a primary heir is insolvent.

Nafaqah Distribution Among Inheriting Relatives
States Hanafi rulings on nafaqah distribution among inheriting relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the distribution of a child's nafaqah among relatives based on their inheritance shares and apply it to scenarios involving different types of sisters and aunts.

Core Idea
Nafaqah for a child with a poor father falls on those relatives who would inherit, in proportion to their shares.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a nuanced Hanafi ruling: when a child's father is poor, the obligation of nafaqah shifts to relatives who would inherit from the child. The distribution is not equal but follows the same proportional shares as inheritance. This lesson walks through the key scenarios: a male child with three types of sisters, and a female child with aunts, highlighting the school's specific positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base principle
The nafaqah of a child with a poor father falls on relatives who would inherit from the child. The father is treated as non-existent.
This is a unique Hanafi rule: nafaqah follows inheritance, not simple proximity.
2
Parse the given distribution for a male child with three sisters
If a male child has a full sister, a consanguine sister, and a uterine sister, the nafaqah is divided in fifths: 3/5 for the full sister, 1/5 for the consanguine, 1/5 for the uterine. This matches their inheritance shares (fard and radd).
The fractions are derived from inheritance rules: full sister gets 1/2, consanguine gets 1/6, uterine gets 1/6, then radd adjusts to fifths.
3
Apply the rule when the child is male and no sister exists
If the male child has no full sister, the nafaqah falls on the paternal aunt from both parents (full paternal aunt), as she would inherit in the absence of the father.
The phrase 'عندنا' emphasizes this is the Hanafi position, not necessarily shared by other schools.
4
Apply the rule when the child is female (daughter) and father is alive
For a daughter, the nafaqah of her father (i.e., the maintenance owed to the father when he is poor) falls on the full sister, because the full sister inherits as a residuary heir alongside the daughter.
The daughter is not treated as non-existent; her inheritance share does not change the obligation on the sister.
5
Apply the rule when the child is female and father is poor
If the daughter's father is needy, the nafaqah for the daughter falls on the paternal aunt from both parents, as she would inherit from the daughter if the daughter died without issue.
This mirrors the case of the male child: the closest inheriting relative (other than the father) bears the nafaqah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخماساً، ثلاثة أخماسه للأخت لأب وأم وخمسه للأخت لأب، وخمسه للأخت لأم فرضاً ورداً، فالنفقة عليهم بحساب ذلك. ونفقة الولد على الأخت لأب وأم خاصة عندنا لأن الوالد المعسر نجعله كالمعدوم، وعند عدم الوالد ميراث الولد للعمة لأب وأم خاصة عندنا فالنفقة تكون عليها أيضاً. وإذا كان الولد بنتاً فنفقة الأب على الأخت لأب وأم خاصة لأنها وارثة مع البنت فإن الأخوات مع البنات عصبة فلا تجعل البنت كالمعدوم، ولكن لو مات الأب كان نصف ميراثه للبنت والباقي للأخت لأب وأم فكذا النفقة على الأخت لأب وأم، ونفقة البنت على العمة لأب وأم خاصة عندنا لأن الأب المحتاج جعل كالمعدوم. وعند انعدام الولد فميراث البنت يكون للعمة لأب وأم خاصة عندنا فكذا النفقة عليها وتمامه في الذخيرة.
In fifths: three fifths for the sister from father and mother, one fifth for the sister from father, and one fifth for the sister from mother as prescribed share and return. So the maintenance is upon them according to that. And the maintenance of a male child is upon the sister from father and mother specifically in our school because we consider the poor father as non-existent. And when the father is absent, the inheritance of the child is for the paternal aunt from father and mother specifically in our school, so maintenance is upon her as well. And if the child is a daughter, then the maintenance of the father is upon the sister from father and mother specifically because she is an heir with the daughter, since sisters with daughters are residuary heirs; so the daughter is not considered non-existent. But if the father died, half of his inheritance would be for the daughter and the remainder for the sister from father and mother, so likewise maintenance is upon the sister from father and mother. And the maintenance of a daughter is upon the paternal aunt from father and mother specifically in our school because the needy father is considered non-existent. And when the child is absent, the inheritance of the daughter is for the paternal aunt from father and mother specifically in our school, so maintenance is upon her. And its completion is in al-Dhakhira.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on how nafaqah (maintenance) is distributed among relatives when a child has a poor father. The Hanafi principle is that when the father is poor, he is treated as if non-existent, and the nafaqah falls on those relatives who would inherit from the child if the child died. The example given is a child who has three surviving sisters: a sister from both parents (full sister), a sister from the father (consanguine sister), and a sister from the mother (uterine sister). The nafaqah is divided among them in proportion to their inheritance shares: the full sister gets three-fifths, the consanguine sister one-fifth, and the uterine sister one-fifth (the latter two via fard and radd). If the child is a son, the nafaqah is specifically on the full sister (or if no full sister, on the paternal aunt from both parents), because she is the one who inherits in the absence of the father. If the child is a daughter, the nafaqah of the father (i.e., the daughter's father) is on the full sister, because she inherits as a residuary with the daughter. If the father is needy, he is treated as absent, and the nafaqah for the daughter falls on the paternal aunt from both parents, as she would inherit the daughter's property if the daughter died without issue. The passage concludes by referring to al-Dhakhira for further details.

Key Terms
الأخت لأب وأم (al-ukht li-ab wa umm): Full sister: sister sharing both father and mother with the child.
الأخت لأب (al-ukht li-ab): Consanguine sister: sister sharing only father with the child.
الأخت لأم (al-ukht li-umm): Uterine sister: sister sharing only mother with the child.
العمة لأب وأم (al-ammah li-ab wa umm): Paternal aunt from both parents: father's full sister.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and shelter, obligated on certain relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a male child has a poor father and three sisters (full, consanguine, uterine), what is the distribution of nafaqah among them?
A) Equal shares: 1/3 each
B) Full sister 1/2, consanguine 1/4, uterine 1/4
C) Full sister 3/5, consanguine 1/5, uterine 1/5
D) Full sister only, 100%

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a child's father is poor, the nafaqah obligation falls on relatives who would inherit from the child, distributed proportionally to their inheritance shares.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Defining Yasar for Nafaqah on Adult Child: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on definition of yasar for nafaqah on adult child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi definitions of yasar for nafaqah on an adult child and explain the practical difference between the nisab zakat and nisab hirmân al-sadaqa views.

Core Idea
Yasar for nafaqah is defined differently: nisab zakat, nisab hirmân al-sadaqa, or surplus after needs.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into the condition of yasar (wealth) required for a father to be obliged to provide nafaqah for his adult child who is unable to work. The Hanafi school presents multiple definitions of yasar, leading to practical differences in who is obligated. Understanding these definitions and their proofs is crucial for correctly applying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and basic ruling
Determine the act (nafaqah on adult child) and its default ruling (wajib) with conditions (child incapacitated, father has yasar). Note that yasar is the disputed element.
The base ruling is from al-Mabsut: nafaqah is wajib without yasar? But later texts condition it on yasar.
2
List the four positions on yasar
Enumerate the four views: (1) nisab zakat, (2) nisab hirmân al-sadaqa, (3) surplus for a month (for those with assets), (4) daily surplus (for earners). Recognize the two soundest (al-asahh) positions.
The text mentions four aqwal, but focuses on the first two and then Muhammad's view.
3
Trace the proofs (dalil) for each view
For view 1: analogy to zakat (nisab determines wealth). For view 2: analogy to sadaqat al-fitr (both have mu'na). For view 3/4: emphasis on current ability (qudra) in huquq al-ibad.
The qiyas panel will detail the analogy to fitra.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Work through a concrete scenario with different definitions. For example, a man with 150 dirhams in savings but a daily surplus of 2 dirhams. Which view obliges him?
The practical difference is whether non-liquid assets count and whether daily earning surplus triggers obligation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and authority weight
Identify which view is relied upon for fatwa. The text indicates the second view (nisab hirmân al-sadaqa) is preferred by al-Dhakhira and al-Hidayah, but Muhammad's view is considered more just (arfaq) and supported by al-Zayla'i and al-Fath.
The author leans towards the second view but acknowledges the merit of Muhammad's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلم مما ذكرناه أن الولد الكبير داخل تحت القريب المحرم فتجب نفقته على الأب بشرط العجز على رواية المبسوط . وعلى ما ذكره الخصاف في نفقاته فهي على الأب والأم أثلاثاً، ثلثاها على الأب والثلث على الأم . قال في الذخيرة وإذا طلب الابن الكبير العاجز أو الأنثى أن يفرض له القاضي النفقة على الأب أجابه القاضي ويدفع ما فرض لهم إليهم لأن ذلك حقهم ولهم ولاية الاستيفاء .اهـ. فعلى هذا لو قال الأب للولد الكبير أنا أطعمك ولا أدفع إليك شيئاً لا يلتفت إليه، وكذا الحكم في نفقة كل محرم لكن لا يشترط يسار الأب لنفقة الولد الكبير العاجز لأنه كالصغير كما في البدائع. وشرط المصنف اليسار لأن الفقير لا تجب عليه نفقة غير الأصول والفروع والزوجة واختلف في حد اليسار على أربعة أقوال مروية، الأصح منها قولان: أحدهما أنه مقدر بنصاب الزكاة قال في الخلاصة : حتى لو انتقص منه درهم لا تجب وبه يفتى واختاره الولوالجي معللاً بأن النفقة تجب على الموسر ونهاية اليسار لا حد لها وبدايته النصاب فيقدر به .اهـ. وثانيهما أنه نصاب حرمان الصدقة رأيته في الذخيرة وكبير (زمن وهو الصواب لأن الصغير المعسر تجب نفقته على الرحم المحرم بلا قيد زمانة، أما الكبير فلا بد منها كما مر ، والظاهر أن الواو في عبارة الذخيرة بمعنى «أو» قوله : (ولهذا المعسر) أي الذي هو أبو الصغير قوله : (على العم والأم خاصة كذا رأيته في نسختي الذخيرة والظاهر أن فيه سقطاً والأصل على العم للأب والأم بقرينة ما بعده. قوله : (وعلى ما ذكره الخصاف في نفقاته الخ) قد تقدم أن ظاهر الرواية الأول قوله : (واختاره الولوالجي الخ) كذا قال في الفتح. قال الرملي : عبارة الولوالجي : ولا يجبر الرجل على نفقة ذوي الرحم المحرم وكان له كفاف وفضل عن قوته حتى يكون له مائتا درهم فصاعداً لأن نفقة ذوي الرحم المحرم تجب على الموسر ونهاية اليسار لا حد لها وبداية اليسار لها حد وهو النصاب فيقدر اليسار بالنصاب ا هـ كلامه وأقول : النصاب في كلامه مطلق محتمل لهذا ولهذا ولا يعينه للزكاة  كان في الهداية وعليه الفتوى وصححه في الذخيرة لأنه لم يشترط لوجوب صدقة الفطر غني موجب الزكاة وإنما شرط غني محرم للصدقة، فكذا حق إيجاب النفقة لأن النفقة بصدقة الفطر أشبه منها بالزكاة لأن في صدقة الفطر معنى المؤنة ومعنى الصدقة، فإذا لم يشترط لوجوب صدقة الفطر غني موجب للزكاة وفي صدقة من وجه مؤنة من وجه فلأن لا يشترط لوجوب النفقة موجب للزكاة وأنها مؤنة من كل وجه كان أولى اهـ. ورجح الزيلعي رواية محمد التي قدرت اليسار بما يفضل عن نفقة نفسه وعياله شهراً إن من أهل الغلة، وإن كان . من أهل الحرف فهو مقدر بما يفضل عن نفقته ونفقة عياله كل لأن المعتبر في حقوق العباد القدرة دون النصاب وهو مستغن عما زاد على ذلك فيصرفه إلى أقاربه إذ المعتبر في حقوق العباد القدرة دون النصاب وهذا أوجه اهـ. وفي التحفة: وقول محمد أرفق وفي غاية البيان ومال شمس الأئمة السرخسي إلى قول محمد اهـ. ولم أر من أفتى به من مشايخنا فالاعتماد على القولين الأولين والأرجح الثاني كما لا يخفى، وقدمنا أن القول لمنكر اليسار والبيئة لمدعيه. وفي القنية : له عم وجد أبو الأم فنفقته على أبي الأم وإن كان الميراث للعم، ولو كان له أم وأب لأم موسران فعلى الأم وفيه إشكال قوي لأنه ذكر في الكتاب إذا كان له أم وعم موسران فالنفقة عليهما أثلاثاً فلم يجعل الأم أقرب من العم، يوم قوله وفضل من قوته لاشتراط النماء فيه فالنصاب مطلق في كلامه فكيف يصح قوله واختاره الولوالجي» تأمل ا هـ . قلت : لكن قوله «حتى يكون له مائتا درهم فصاعداً يعين نصاب الزكاة إذا لو كان المراد نصاب حرمان الصدقة لقال حتى يكون له ما يساوي مائتي درهم ولو غير نام إذ لا شك أن المائتين من الدراهم نصاب نام فهو نصاب الزكاة لا نصاب حرمانها قوله : (ورجح الزيلعي رواية محمد التي قدرت الخ) وكذا رجحها في الفتح حيث قال : وإذا كان كسوباً يعتبر قول محمد، وهذا يجب أن يعول عليه في الفتوى ا.هـ. وأشار بقوله «التي الخ» إلى عن محمد روايتين. قال في الفتح : وعن محمد روايتان أحدهما بما يفضل عن نفقة شهر والأخرى بما يفضل عن كسبه كل يوم حتى لو كان كسبه درهماً ويكفيه أربعة دوانق وجب عليه الدانقان للقريب ومحمل الروايتين على حاجة الإنسان إن كان مكتسباً لا مال له حاصل اعتبر فضل كسبه اليومي وإن لم يكن بل له مال اعتبر نفقة شهر فينفق ذلك الشهر فإن صار فقيراً ارتفعت نفقته عنه ا هـ . فما ذكره المؤلف هو محمل الروايتين لا أحدهما كما يوهمه ظاهر كلامه وبما ذكر عن الفتح تتم الأقوال الأربعة تأمل قوله: (وفيه إشكال قوي الخ) قال الرملي يمكن أن يقال الأم مع الجد أبي الأم مع كونها أقرب منه فاجتمع فيها الأرث والأقربية . معه بخلافها مع العم لوجود الإرث فيهما فاعتبر أي الأرث تأمل قوله : (إذا كان له أم وعم موسران فالنففة عليهما قال الرملي فلو كانا معسرين فهي على الأم لا على العم لما تقدم من قوله الفقير لا تجب عليه نفقة غير الأصول والفروع والأم من قسم الأصول لا العم . هي وارثة
[See commentary for translation as it is lengthy]
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the father must be wealthy (yasar) to be obliged to provide nafaqah for an adult child (who is unable to work). The text states that according to al-Mabsut, the adult child's nafaqah is obligatory on the father if the child is incapable (عاجز). Al-Khassaf reports another opinion: the nafaqah is divided between father and mother in thirds (two-thirds on father, one-third on mother). Al-Dhakhira mentions that the father can be forced to pay, and if he offers to feed but not hand over money, the father's offer is ignored. The author (of Kanz al-Daqaiq) stipulated yasar (wealth) because a poor person is not required to spend on non-lineal relatives. Then the text details the ikhtilaf on defining yasar: four views, two most sound: (1) it is measured by nisab al-zakat (200 dirhams of silver) – this is the fatwa and chosen by al-Walwaliji; (2) it is measured by nisab hirmân al-sadaqa (niṣāb that prohibits receiving charity) – this is considered correct in al-Dhakhira. The third view is from Imam Muhammad: yasar is surplus after meeting one's own and family's needs for a month (if one has produce) or daily surplus (if one earns). Al-Zayla'i and al-Fath preferred this view for the kasib (earner). But the author says the first two views are relied upon, with the second being more likely. The passage also discusses a case: if a person has a mother and a maternal grandfather (who is the father of the mother), nafaqah is on the maternal grandfather even though inheritance goes to the uncle. If both mother and paternal uncle are wealthy, nafaqah is on both in thirds? Actually the text discusses a difficulty: if a child has a mother and paternal uncle, the nafaqah is on both in thirds, not just on the mother despite her being closer? But then it explains that if both are poor, nafaqah is on the mother because she is an ascendant (أصل). The overall discussion shows the complexity of nafaqah obligations among relatives.

Key Terms
اليسار (al-yasar): Wealth or financial ability that obligates nafaqah on a person for relatives beyond immediate family.
نصاب الزكاة (nisab al-zakat): Minimum amount of wealth (200 dirhams of silver or equivalent) that makes zakat obligatory.
نصاب حرمان الصدقة (nisab hirmân al-sadaqa): Amount of wealth that prohibits a person from receiving zakat or charity (i.e., being considered rich).
الولد الكبير العاجز (al-walad al-kabir al-'ajiz): Adult child who is unable to work due to disability or other legitimate incapacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view on the definition of yasar is described as 'arfaq' (more just) and is supported by al-Zayla'i and al-Fath for the case of an earner (kasib)?
A) Nisab zakat
B) Nisab hirmân al-sadaqa
C) Surplus after monthly needs (for farmers) or daily surplus (for earners)
D) Surplus after one year's expenses

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school offers multiple definitions of yasar for nafaqah on an adult child, with the second (nisab hirmân al-sadaqa) being the mufta bihi, but Muhammad's surplus-based view is more equitable for earners.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate

The Nisab for Obligatory Maintenance: Surplus, Not Zakat Threshold
Determining the nisab for maintenance obligation, comparing to zakat and sadaqat al-fitr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school uses a different wealth standard for maintenance than for zakat, and apply the preferred standard to determine when maintenance is obligatory.

Core Idea
Maintenance is tied to surplus after needs, not zakat's fixed nisab.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into the legal reasoning behind the nisab (wealth threshold) for obligatory maintenance (nafaqah). It distinguishes the condition for maintenance from that of zakat by drawing an analogy to sadaqat al-fitr. The author also presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement and concludes with the relied-upon position. By the end, you will understand why the standard is based on surplus after needs, not a fixed amount.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Disputed Point
First, note that the obligation of maintenance is wajib when the person has yasar (wealth sufficiency). The dispute is about the exact definition of yasar: is it the same as the threshold that prohibits accepting sadaqah (ghani muharram al-sadaqah) or is it based on practical ability (qudra) as measured by surplus after needs?
2
Locate the Hanafi Proof (Dalil)
The Hanafi proof is an istihsan (juristic preference) based on an analogy with sadaqat al-fitr. The condition for sadaqat al-fitr is not wealth that necessitates zakat, but wealth that prohibits sadaqah. Since maintenance resembles sadaqat al-fitr (both have elements of support and charity), the same condition applies.
The passage explicitly states 'because maintenance is more similar to sadaqat al-fitr than to zakat'.
3
Trace the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Within the Hanafi school, Imam Muhammad (as narrated by al-Zayla'i) held that yasar should be measured by surplus after one's own and family's needs for a month (for agricultural earners) or daily (for craftsmen). This view emphasizes ability (qudra) over a fixed nisab.
The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that yasar is the same as the condition for sadaqat al-fitr, i.e., wealth that prohibits accepting charity.
4
Evaluate the Tarjih (Preponderance)
The author notes that although some later scholars (like al-Sarakhsi) preferred Muhammad's view, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the first two sayings, with the second (the correction from al-Dhakhirah) being more preponderant. The practical ability approach is 'more appropriate' for rights of people, but the fatwa remains on the majority view.
The passage says 'الاعتماد على القولين الأولين والأرجح الثاني' (reliance on the first two sayings, and the second is more preponderant).
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The difference matters in a scenario where a person has wealth that exceeds the sadaqah threshold but after deducting his and his family's monthly expenses, there is no surplus. According to the majority view, maintenance is still obligatory because the person is considered 'ghani' for sadaqah. According to Muhammad's view, it is not obligatory due to lack of actual surplus. The fatwa follows the majority view, but with a leaning toward the surplus measure.
The passage concludes with a procedural note: the one denying yasar is believed, and the burden of proof is on the claimant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان في وهو النصاب الذي ليس .بنام. قال في الهداية وعليه الفتوى وصححه في الذخيرة لأنه لم يشترط لوجوب صدقة الفطر غني موجب الزكاة وإنما شرط غني محرم للصدقة، فكذا حق إيجاب النفقة لأن النفقة بصدقة الفطر أشبه منها بالزكاة لأن في صدقة الفطر معنى المؤنة ومعنى الصدقة، فإذا لم يشترط لوجوب صدقة الفطر غني موجب للزكاة وفي صدقة من وجه مؤنة من وجه فلأن لا يشترط لوجوب النفقة موجب للزكاة وأنها مؤنة من كل وجه كان أولى اهـ. ورجح الزيلعي رواية محمد التي قدرت اليسار بما يفضل عن نفقة نفسه وعياله شهراً إن من أهل الغلة، وإن كان . من أهل الحرف فهو مقدر بما يفضل عن نفقته ونفقة عياله كل لأن المعتبر في حقوق العباد القدرة دون النصاب وهو مستغن عما زاد على ذلك فيصرفه إلى أقاربه إذ المعتبر في حقوق العباد القدرة دون النصاب وهذا أوجه اهـ. وفي التحفة: وقول محمد أرفق وفي غاية البيان ومال شمس الأئمة السرخسي إلى قول محمد اهـ. ولم أر من أفتى به من مشايخنا فالاعتماد على القولين الأولين والأرجح الثاني كما لا يخفى، وقدمنا أن القول لمنكر اليسار والبيئة لمدعيه.
It was said: the nisab which is not growing. He said in al-Hidayah: and the fatwa is upon it, and it was corrected in al-Dhakhirah because for the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr, wealth that necessitates zakat was not stipulated, but rather wealth that prohibits sadaqah was stipulated. So likewise the right of imposing maintenance because maintenance is more similar to sadaqat al-fitr than to zakat, because in sadaqat al-fitr there is the meaning of maintenance and the meaning of charity. So when for the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr wealth necessitating zakat is not stipulated, and it is charity from one aspect and maintenance from another, then for the obligation of maintenance, which is maintenance from every aspect, it is more appropriate not to stipulate wealth necessitating zakat. End. And al-Zayla'i preferred the narration of Muhammad which measured yasar by what remains after the maintenance of oneself and one's family for a month if he is from people of produce, and if he is from people of crafts, it is measured by what remains after his own maintenance and his family's maintenance every day because consideration in the rights of people is ability, not nisab, and he is independent of what is beyond that, so he spends it on his relatives. And this is more appropriate, end. And in al-Tuhfah: Muhammad's saying is more lenient, and in Ghayat al-Bayan, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi inclined to Muhammad's saying, end. And I have not seen anyone from our scholars giving fatwa according to it, so reliance is on the first two sayings, and the second is more preponderant as is not hidden. And we previously stated that the statement is denied by the one denying yasar, and the evidence is upon the claimant.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute about the standard of wealth (nisab) that triggers the obligation of maintenance for relatives. The author begins by presenting the position from al-Hidayah that the nisab for maintenance is not the zakat threshold but rather the threshold that prohibits accepting charity (sadaqah). This is based on an analogy (istihsan) comparing maintenance to sadaqat al-fitr, which combines aspects of both maintenance and charity. Al-Zayla'i then prefers the narration of Muhammad that measures wealth by what remains after one's own and one's family's needs for a month (if one has agricultural produce) or daily (if one is a craftsman). The author notes that while some scholars like al-Sarakhsi preferred Muhammad's view, the relied-upon position is still the first two sayings (the one from al-Hidayah and the corrected one from al-Dhakhirah), with the second being more preponderant. The passage ends with a procedural note that the one denying wealth (munkir al-yasar) is believed, and the burden of proof lies on the claimant.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisāb): The minimum wealth threshold that triggers the obligation of maintenance.
يسار (yasār): Wealth sufficiency; having surplus beyond one's own and family's needs.
غني موجب الزكاة (ghanī mūjib al-zakāh): Wealth that necessitates payment of zakat.
غني محرم الصدقة (ghanī muḥarram al-ṣadaqah): Wealth that prohibits accepting sadaqah (i.e., the threshold above which one is not eligible to receive zakat/sadaqah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because both are purely charitable.
B) Because both involve a fixed nisab.
C) Because sadaqat al-fitr has both the meaning of maintenance and charity.
D) Because maintenance requires a larger wealth.

Key Takeaway
The nisab for maintenance is surplus after needs, based on analogy to sadaqat al-fitr, not a fixed zakat threshold.

Maintenance for a Child with Multiple Relatives: Resolving an Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Resolving an intra-Hanafi dispute on maintenance for a child with multiple relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the apparent inconsistency in Hanafi rulings on maintenance when a child has a mother, paternal uncle, and maternal grandfather, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Maintenance liability follows inheritance shares, not merely closeness of lineage.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a challenging case in Hanafi fiqh: when a child has multiple relatives—mother, paternal uncle, and maternal grandfather—who is responsible for maintenance? The rulings seem contradictory at first glance. The author highlights a 'strong problem' and offers a resolution that balances closeness and inheritance rights. By understanding this dispute, you will learn how Hanafi jurists harmonize conflicting principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three basic cases
The passage presents three scenarios: (a) child with paternal uncle and maternal grandfather, (b) child with mother and maternal grandfather, and (c) child with mother and paternal uncle. For each, note the ruling given.
These are the building blocks for the tripartite case.
2
Spot the inconsistency
In (b) the mother alone is liable; in (c) mother and uncle share equally (thirds). Yet the maternal grandfather is closer than the uncle, so why is the mother bearing the full burden in (b) while sharing in (c)? This is the 'strong problem'.
The principle of closeness (qurb) seems to lead to contradictory results.
3
Understand the role of inheritance
The resolution appeals to inheritance shares. In Hanafi law, the mother inherits a fixed share (1/6 with a child), the uncle is a residuary, and the maternal grandfather is also a residuary but is excluded by the mother when present. Thus, when mother and uncle are present, they inherit; when mother and maternal grandfather are present, only the mother inherits.
Maintenance follows inheritance: those who inherit bear maintenance in proportion to their shares.
4
Apply the tripartite case
When all three are present, the mother excludes the maternal grandfather from inheritance, leaving mother and uncle as heirs. Therefore, maintenance is on them in thirds, matching the ruling for case (c). This is the preferred resolution.
The author states: 'ويحتمل أن يكون على الأم والعم أثلاثاً' (it is probable that it is upon the mother and uncle in thirds).
5
Confirm with a parallel ruling
The passage cites al-Khaniyah: for a child with a mother and paternal grandfather (father's father), maintenance is in thirds (1/3 mother, 2/3 grandfather). This shows that grandfather is not treated as a father, and his share aligns with his inheritance.
This reinforces the principle that inheritance shares determine maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : له عم وجد أبو الأم فنفقته على أبي الأم وإن كان الميراث للعم، ولو كان له أم وأب لأم موسران فعلى الأم وفيه إشكال قوي لأنه ذكر في الكتاب إذا كان له أم وعم موسران فالنفقة عليهما أثلاثاً فلم يجعل الأم أقرب من العم، وجعل في المسألة المتقدمة أب الأم أقرب من العم ولزم منه أن تكون النفقة على أب الأم مع هذا أوجبها على الأم. ويتفرع من هذه الجملة فرع أشكل الجواب فيه وهو ما إذا كانت له أم وعم وأب لأم موسرون فيحتمل أن تجب على الأم لا غير لأن أبا الأم لما كان أولى من العم والأم أولى من أبي الأم كانت الأم أولى من العم لكن بترك جواب الكتاب، ويحتمل أن يكون على الأم والعم أثلاثاً اهـ. وفي الخانية صغير مات أبوه وله أم وجد أب الأب كانت النفقة عليهما أثلاثاً، الثلث على الأم والثلثان على جد الأب اهـ. وبه علم أن الجد ليس كالأب فيها.
And in al-Qunyah: He has a paternal uncle and a maternal grandfather, so his maintenance is upon the maternal grandfather even if inheritance is for the uncle. And if he had a mother and a maternal grandfather both wealthy, then upon the mother, and in it there is a strong problem because he mentioned in the book that if he has a mother and a paternal uncle both wealthy, then maintenance is upon both in thirds, so he did not make the mother closer than the uncle. And he made in the previous issue the maternal grandfather closer than the uncle, and it necessarily follows that maintenance is upon the maternal grandfather, yet he obligated it upon the mother. And from this sentence branches a sub-case whose answer is problematic: that if he has a mother, a paternal uncle, and a maternal grandfather all wealthy, then it is possible that it is obligatory upon the mother alone because the maternal grandfather is more entitled than the uncle, and the mother is more entitled than the maternal grandfather, so the mother is more entitled than the uncle, but this abandons the answer of the book. And it is possible that it is upon the mother and the uncle in thirds. End. And in al-Khaniyah: A minor whose father dies and he has a mother and a paternal grandfather (father's father), maintenance is upon both in thirds: one third on the mother and two thirds on the paternal grandfather. End. And by that it is known that the grandfather is not like the father in this matter.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents a intra-Hanafi dispute regarding maintenance liability for a child who has multiple relatives. It begins with a case from al-Qunyah: if a child has a paternal uncle and a maternal grandfather, maintenance falls on the maternal grandfather even though inheritance goes to the uncle. This is because the maternal grandfather is closer in lineage. The passage then presents a 'strong problem': if the child has a mother and a maternal grandfather, maintenance is on the mother, but earlier it was stated that if a child has a mother and a paternal uncle, maintenance is split in thirds. This seems inconsistent because the maternal grandfather is closer than the uncle, so why is the mother bearing the full burden? The resolution proposed is that when mother, uncle, and maternal grandfather are all present, maintenance should be on the mother and uncle in thirds, as per the principle of inheritance shares. The passage also cites al-Khaniyah: if a child has a mother and a paternal grandfather (father's father), maintenance is in thirds: one third on mother, two thirds on grandfather. This shows that the grandfather is not treated like the father in these calculations.

Key Terms
أب الأم (ab al-umm): The maternal grandfather (father of the mother).
عم (amm): Paternal uncle (father's brother).
إشكال قوي (ishkāl qawī): A strong problem or inconsistency in the rulings.
الخراج بالضمان (al-kharāj bi al-ḍamān): A legal maxim: 'Profit is accompanied by liability' — in this context, maintenance follows the right to inherit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the mother is obligated.
B) Only the maternal grandfather is obligated.
C) The mother and uncle are obligated in thirds.
D) All three are obligated in equal shares.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, maintenance liability for a child among multiple relatives is determined by inheritance shares, not merely by closeness of lineage.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Sale of Child's Property for Maintenance and Distribution Among Relatives
Ruling on sale of child's property and distribution of maintenance among relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a child's property for maintenance and apply the principles of closeness and inheritance to determine who bears maintenance when multiple relatives are present.

Core Idea
A father may sell only movable property of his child for maintenance; when multiple relatives share obligation, nearness in lineage takes precedence unless all are inheritors, then shares by inheritance.

In this passage, we learn a specific ruling: a father may sell his child's movable property (but not real estate) for the child's maintenance. The text then branches into a more complex scenario where a child has multiple relatives (mother, paternal uncle, maternal grandfather) each capable of providing maintenance. The Hanafi scholars discuss how to allocate the obligation, weighing closeness of kinship against inheritance rights. This lesson will help you understand both the basic ruling and the nuanced application when multiple potential maintainers exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on selling property
The text states that the sale of a child's movable property (ʿaraḍ) for maintenance is valid, but not real estate (ʿiqār). Understand the distinction: only movable goods can be sold to cover maintenance costs.
This is an exception to the general prohibition on selling a child's property; it is permitted out of necessity.
2
Examine the branch case with multiple relatives
A problematic branch arises: a child has a mother, a paternal uncle, and a maternal grandfather, all wealthy. The question is who must provide maintenance? The text presents two possibilities: either the mother alone, or the mother and paternal uncle in thirds.
The maternal grandfather is excluded because he is not a mahram and is further in lineage.
3
Apply the principles of closeness and inheritance
Hanafi jurists generally prioritize the closest relative (in terms of mahram relationship). However, when multiple relatives are all inheritors, maintenance is divided according to inheritance shares. Here, the mother and paternal uncle both inherit, but the maternal grandfather is excluded by the mother.
Al-Ramli prefers the view that maintenance is on the mother and paternal uncle in thirds, as each is an inheritor and the grandfather is treated as if dead.
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction with earlier principles
The author of the base text (al-Kanz) originally stated that only closeness matters, not inheritance. Al-Ramli argues that this branch case is an exception because all relatives involved are inheritors. Thus, when all are inheritors, the earlier rule (closeness) is overridden by inheritance shares.
This shows the importance of context: general principles may have exceptions in specific configurations.

على الأم خاصة لا على الخال ولا على أولاد العم؛ أما الخال فإنه لا أرث له مع الأم مع كونها أقرب . منه فلا وجه لاشتراكه في النفقة بخلاف العم فإنه يرث معها، يدل عليه ما في كتبهم عامة من عدم مشاركة أب الأم علم الخ) قال الرملي قال في التتارخانية نقلاً عن المحيط : تجب عليهما أثلاثاً
And the sale of his son's movable property, not real estate, for maintenance is valid. And if a depositary spends on his parents without permission, he is liable. And if he spends... (then) And from this sentence branches a sub-case whose answer is problematic: that if he has a mother, a paternal uncle, and a maternal grandfather all wealthy, then it is possible that it is obligatory upon the mother alone... (etc.) And in al-Khaniyah: A minor whose father dies and he has a mother and a paternal grandfather, maintenance is upon both in thirds: one third on the mother and two thirds on the paternal grandfather. End. And by that it is known that the grandfather is not like the father in this matter. His saying: (And the sale of his son's movable property, not real estate, for maintenance is valid, and analogically it is not permissible for him to sell anything, and he is saying:) (And from this sentence branches...) Al-Ramli said: I say: When grandfathers and grandmothers gather, the nearest is obligated, even if the other does not indicate due to his closeness. And in al-Fayd al-Kurki: And when they are equal in mahramiyya, the one who truly inherits in this case is preferred, even if he has a maternal uncle and a paternal uncle, maintenance is on the paternal uncle. So likewise if he has a maternal aunt and a paternal aunt, maintenance is on the paternal uncle. And if the paternal uncle is poor, then maintenance is on the paternal aunt and maternal aunt in thirds according to their inheritance, and the paternal uncle is treated as dead. End. And it appears from their branches that closeness is only given precedence when they are not all inheritors. But if they are all inheritors, then it is not, like the mother and the paternal uncle or the grandfather, according to their saying: according to inheritance. And what should be relied upon in the problematic branch is that maintenance is on the mother and the paternal uncle in thirds, because each of them is an inheritor and the maternal grandfather has been removed by the mother, so he is like dead. So ponder, the matter will become clear to you. I say: This contradicts what the author previously stated from his saying 'And he indicated by his saying 'wa li-abawayhi' that consideration in the obligation of maintenance of parents and children is only closeness and partiality, and inheritance is not considered...' So refer back and ponder. Then al-Ramli said: And I was asked about an orphan girl who has a mother, a maternal uncle, and paternal cousins. I answered that her maintenance is on the mother alone, not on the maternal uncle nor on the paternal cousins. As for the maternal uncle, he has no inheritance with the mother while she is closer, so there is no basis for his sharing in maintenance. Unlike the paternal uncle, he inherits with her. This is indicated by their general books that the maternal grandfather does not share with her, so how about the maternal uncle? Because if the maternal grandfather gathered with the maternal uncle, the maternal grandfather is given precedence without doubt. So we know definitively that the maternal uncle has nothing of maintenance, and the mother has more right. And what indicates it is what is in al-Minhaj al-Hanafi from his saying: If a minor has a wealthy mother and a wealthy grandfather and no father, then his maintenance is on the mother and the grandfather according to their inheritance shares, and likewise the paternal uncle with the mother, and likewise the rest of the asaba besides them with her. And if a minor has a wealthy paternal cousin and a wealthy maternal uncle, his maintenance is on his maternal uncle. End. So its implication is that other than the asaba with her do not share with her, and the maternal uncle is not asaba, so he does not share with her. Whoever assumes that has deviated far from understanding. I mentioned that because I found some muftis of this era giving fatwa accordingly. And it has preceded that if the paternal uncle and maternal uncle gather, maintenance is on the paternal uncle, so a fortiori if the maternal uncle gathers with the mother, he has nothing, as previous in the face of the problem. As for the paternal cousin, there is no maintenance on him even if alone, because he is not a mahram. And Allah knows best. His saying: (on the paternal grandfather) correct is on the grandfather, the father's father, because the pronoun in 'lahu' refers to the minor. His saying: (And by that it is known...) Al-Ramli said in al-Tatar khaniyyah quoting from al-Muhit: It is obligatory on both in thirds.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with additional examples and intra-Hanafi discussion about the sale of a child's property for maintenance and the distribution of maintenance among multiple relatives. It begins by stating that a father may sell his child's movable property (but not real estate) for maintenance. Then it branches into a problematic case involving a child with a mother, paternal uncle, and maternal grandfather. The commentary by al-Ramli provides further principles: when multiple relatives are present, the nearest in lineage is obligated unless all are inheritors, in which case maintenance is according to inheritance shares. Al-Ramli also recounts a fatwa about an orphan girl with a mother, maternal uncle, and paternal cousins: the mother alone bears maintenance. The maternal uncle is not obligated because he is neither an heir nor a mahram. The passage concludes with a correction regarding the grandfather's terminology.

Key Terms
عرض (ʿaraḍ): Movable property, goods other than real estate.
عقار (ʿiqār): Real estate, land, buildings.
مودع (mūdiʿ): Depositary; someone entrusted with property (wadi'a).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is true about maintenance for a minor?
A) A father may sell his child's real estate for maintenance if necessary.
B) An orphan with a mother and a maternal uncle: maintenance is on both.
C) An orphan with a mother and a paternal uncle: maintenance is divided by inheritance.
D) An orphan with a mother and a maternal grandfather: maintenance is on the mother alone.

Key Takeaway
A father may sell only his child's movable property for maintenance; when multiple relatives are obligated, nearness in lineage is primary unless all are inheritors, in which case maintenance is apportioned according to inheritance shares.

Priority of Relatives for Nafaqah in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on distribution of nafaqah among multiple relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for distributing nafaqah among multiple relatives based on closeness and inheritance shares.

Core Idea
When relatives are equal in closeness, distribution follows inheritance shares; otherwise, the closest is prioritized.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq presents a difficult branch case: when a minor has a mother, a paternal uncle, and a maternal uncle or grandfather, all wealthy, who is liable for nafaqah? The Hanafi scholars derive a nuanced rule combining closeness and inheritance. This lesson unpacks the principles from the text, including the crucial ruling that when all are heirs, distribution is per inheritance shares, and the closer relative excludes the more distant only if the closer is not a full heir.

Guided Path
1
Identify all relatives and their closeness
List the relatives: mother, paternal uncle, maternal uncle (or maternal grandfather). Determine their degree of kinship (mahram or not) and their closeness to the child.
The text uses 'ap-um' (mother), 'am' (paternal uncle), 'ab li-um' (maternal grandfather).
2
Apply the closeness rule
If one relative is closer (e.g., mother is closer than maternal grandfather), that relative is obligated alone unless others are heirs of equal degree.
The author notes that maternal grandfather is closer than paternal uncle, but mother is closest.
3
Check if all are heirs
If all present relatives are heirs of the minor, closeness alone does not determine; rather, distribution is based on inheritance shares (mirath).
The text states: 'الأقربية إنما تقدم إذا لم يكونوا وارثين كلهم' (closeness is preferred only when they are not all heirs).
4
Determine shares when all are heirs
In the branch case, mother and paternal uncle are both heirs. Maternal uncle is not an agnate heir with mother present, so he is treated as dead (like the maternal grandfather). Thus, nafaqah is on mother and paternal uncle in proportion to their inheritance shares: one-third and two-thirds respectively.
The preferred ruling from al-Ramli: mother gets 1/3, paternal uncle gets 2/3.
5
Consider other scenarios
If relatives are not all heirs, the closest alone is liable. For example, with only a maternal uncle and a paternal uncle present (no mother), the paternal uncle is liable because he is an agnate heir.
The text cites al-Minhaj al-Hanafi: if minor has a wealthy maternal uncle and a wealthy paternal male cousin, nafaqah is on maternal uncle because he is a mahram, not on paternal cousin who is not mahram.
