Older Brother's Liability When Spending from Younger Brother's Inheritance
Ruling with internal Hanafi tension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general rule of liability and the exceptional permissibility for an older brother spending from a younger brother's inheritance, and state the conditions that affect the ruling.

Core Idea
Without guardianship, a brother is liable for spending a minor's inheritance; only necessary maintenance may be permitted.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a tension within the Hanafi school: the general rule from al-Khassaf holds the older brother liable for spending from his younger brother's inheritance, while a statement in al-Jami' al-Saghir suggests permissibility. We will parse the two positions and understand how Hanafi jurists reconcile them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting sources
Locate the two authorities: al-Khassaf (liability) and al-Jami' al-Saghir (permissibility). Note the page reference and the nature of each source (a treatise on nafaqah vs. a chapter on karahiyya).
This is an intra-Hanafi text contradiction.
2
State the general rule of liability
From Nafaqat al-Khassaf: the older brother is liable (yadman) because he lacks guardianship (wilayah) over the minor brother. This is the default position.
The text explicitly says 'عليه يضمن في الحكم لأنه لا ولاية له عليه'.
3
Identify the apparent exception
From al-Jami' al-Saghir: it is written that he is permitted (yamliku al-infaq) to spend. This seems to contradict the general rule.
The commentator uses 'يكتب' (it is written) to indicate a reported view.
4
Analyze the reconciliations
Two possible interpretations: (a) the permission is limited to spending on actual maintenance like food, where he does not need to sell property; (b) the brother is the de facto guardian (fi hijrihi) and the money is cash, so he purchases necessities. Both narrow the exception.
The commentator uses 'يحتمل' to show these are possibilities, not certainties.
5
Determine the operative ruling
The general rule of liability is the stronger position; the exception is limited to specific circumstances. The mufta bihi is that the brother is liable unless he can prove the narrow conditions.
This reflects the Hanafi preference for the established riwayah (al-Khassaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وكذا الورثة الكبار الخ ذكر في نفقات الخصاف الأخ الكبير مع الأخ الصغير إذا ورثا مالاً وفي البلد قاض أو لم يكن فأنفق الأخ من نصيب الأخ الصغير عليه يضمن في الحكم لأنه لا ولاية له عليه. وكتبت في آخر كراهية الجامع الصغير ما يدل على أنه يملك الإنفاق فيحتمل أن تأويل ما ذكر في الجامع الصغير الإنفاق من جنس النفقة من طعام ،وغيره وفي هذا لا يحتاج إلى بيع الأخ ، ويحتمل أن الأخ في حجره والمال دراهم ويحتاج إلى شراء ما لا بد منه وهو النفقة
He said: (Similarly the adult inheritors...). Mentioned in Nafaqat al-Khassaf: The older brother with the younger brother if they inherited wealth and there is a judge in the town or not, and the older brother spent from the younger brother's share – he is liable in legal judgment because he has no guardianship over him. But it is written at the end of Karahiyya of al-Jami' al-Saghir that which indicates that he is permitted to spend. It is possible that the interpretation of what is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir is spending on the type of maintenance like food and other things, and in this he does not need sell the brother's property; and it is possible that the brother is in his guardianship (hijr) and the wealth is dirhams, and he needs to buy what is necessary, which is maintenance.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage discusses the case of an older brother who spends from the inheritance of his younger brother (a minor) when they both inherit. The general ruling is that the older brother is liable (يضمن) in legal judgment because he lacks guardianship (ولاية) over the younger brother. However, the author notes a possible exception from al-Jami' al-Saghir, which suggests that the older brother may have the right to spend. The reconciliation proposed is that the permissibility may be limited to spending on actual maintenance (like food) without needing to sell property, or it may apply when the older brother is the de facto guardian (hijr) and the property is in cash, so he purchases necessities. This shows a tension within the Hanafi sources: the general rule from al-Khassaf imposes liability, while al-Jami' al-Saghir implies permissibility. The commentator suggests possible interpretations to resolve the conflict, but leaves it as a probability (يحتمل).

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hijr): Guardianship or custody; the authority to manage a minor's affairs.
الولاية (al-wilayah): Legal authority or guardianship to act on behalf of another.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to compensate or guarantee; here, the obligation to repay the amount spent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the core reason the older brother is liable for spending from his younger brother's inheritance?
A) Because the younger brother is a minor and cannot consent.
B) Because the older brother has no legal guardianship (wilayah).
C) Because there is a judge in the town.
D) Because the inheritance is in cash.

Key Takeaway
An older brother is liable for spending a minor brother's inheritance unless he has guardianship or the spending is strictly necessary maintenance under de facto custody.

Maintenance of Dependents: Immediate Family vs. Other Relatives
Commentary on rulings of spending on dependents and necessity of court order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which dependents may take maintenance without court order and state the Hanafi proof for the obligation of maintenance before qada'.

Core Idea
Immediate family’s maintenance is wajib before any court order; other relatives need a decree or consent.

This lesson unpacks a key distinction in Hanafi nafaqah: the maintenance of parents, children, and spouse is obligatory (wajib) even before a judge rules on it, so they may lawfully take their due without permission. Other relatives, though the obligation exists in principle, cannot enforce it until a court orders or they consent. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the reasoning, the proof from the Quran, and the role of ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of dependents
From the text, list which relatives are considered 'immediate family' (parents, children, spouse) and which are 'other relatives' (e.g., siblings, uncles). Note the different legal consequences.
The commentary explicitly states: 'نفقة الوالدين والمولودين والزوجة واجبة قبل القضاء' while for others 'لا تجب إلا بالقضاء أو الرضا'.
2
Extract the distinction between wujūb and luzūm
Understand that for other relatives, the obligation (wujūb) is established in principle by the Quran, but it is not binding (lāzim) for performance until a judge decrees or the obligee consents. This is due to ikhtilaf among scholars.
Al-Ramli: 'معنى قولهم لا تجب أي لا تلزم إلا بالقضاء وإن كانت واجبة قبله'.
3
Analyze the proof: Quran 2:233
The verse 'وعلى الوارث مثل ذلك' is cited. 'على' indicates obligation in Hanafi usul. Explain how this establishes the underlying wujūb for relatives, but the ikhtilaf affects enforcement.
The judge's decree is 'إعانة' (assistance), not creation of obligation.
4
Apply the rule: practical scenarios
Test scenarios: a wife taking food from her husband's property without consent is permissible; a needy brother cannot take from his brother's property without court order or consent because he is not immediate family.
The text gives the example of the father spending on himself from son's property: if father is insolvent, he is not liable by istihsan.
5
Distinguish legal liability (ḥukman) from religious responsibility (diyānatan)
The commentary shows that even when one is legally liable (e.g., executor spending without authority), religiously there is no sin if the intention was reform (istihsan). Understand the dual aspect.
This distinction is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بالضمان فكان متبرعاً بملك نفسه، وظاهره أنه لا فرق بين أن ينفق عليهم وبين أن يدفع الوديعة إليهم في وجوب الضمان وعدم الرجوع عليهم لوجود العلة فيهما، ولم أر أنه إذا أنفق عليهم بلا أمر ثم أجاز المالك واجبة قبل القضاء على ما مر وقد أخذا جنس الحق. وفي الخلاصة: ولو أنفق على نفسه من مال الابن ثم خاصمه الابن فقال أنفقته وأنت موسر وقال الأب أنفقته وأنا معسر إلى حال الأب يوم الخصومة إن كان معسراً فالقول الخ ذكر في نفقات الخصاف الأخ الكبير مع الأخ الصغير إذا ورثا مالاً وفي البلد قاض أو لم يكن فأنفق الأخ من نصيب الأخ الصغير عليه يضمن في الحكم لأنه لا ولاية له عليه. وكتبت في آخر كراهية الجامع الصغير ما وهو النفقة عندهما لا فلو قضى بنفقة الولاد والقريب ومضت مدة سقطت إلا أن يأذن القاضي بالاستدانة ولمملوكه فإن أبى ففي كسبه وإلا أمره ببيعه . رضا. فأما نفقة سائر الأقارب لا تجب إلا بالقضاء أو الرضا حتى لو ظفروا واحد من الأقارب بجنس حقه لم يكن له الأخذ إلا بقضاء أو رضا، ولذا يفرض القاضي في مال الغائب نفقة الأولين فقط .اهـ. قوله : ( ولو قضى بنفقة الولاد والقريب ومضت مدة سقطت) لأن نفقة هؤلاء تجب كفاية للحاجة حتى لا تجب مع اليسار وقد حصلت الكفاية بمضي المدة بخلاف نفقة الزوجة إذا قضى بها القاضي لأنها تجب مع يسارها فلا تسقط بحصول الاستغناء فيما مضى ولم أر . بأنه يأثم ومقتضى وجوبها أنه يأثم بتركها إذا طلبها صاحبها وامتنع مع أنهم قالوا إنها لا تجب إلا بالقضاء أو الرضا كما قدمناه عن الذخيرة، ولذا ليس الخصاف في أدب القاضي بأنها لا تجب له أن يأخذها بغير قضاء ولا رضا وصرح ) لمن هي من صرح نفقة إلا بالقضاء للاختلاف فيها واستشكله السروجي في الغاية من حيث إنهم جعلوا القاضي نفسه هو الذي أوجب هذه النفقة والقاضي ليس بمشرع وما ذاك إلا للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وانقطع من بعده فهو مشكل جداً. وتبعه على ذلك الطرسوسي في أنفع الوسائل وقال: لم لا؟ قيل: إن الوجوب يثبت بقوله تعالى : (وعلى الوارث مثل ذلك) [البقرة : ۲۳۳] فقضاء القاضي إعانة له كما في نفقة الأولاد، كيف وإنهم قد استدلوا في أصل المسألة بهذه الآية على وجوب القريب وكلمة «على» للإيجاب ولا يعكر على هذا اختلاف العلماء لأن المسائل الاختلافية يعمل فيها على الاختلاف ولا يكون الاختلاف مؤثراً في عدم القبول فإن ذلك كان واجباً قبل القضاء كما قلنا في نفقة المبتوتة أنه يقضي بها باعتبار أنها ثابتة قبل القضاء والقضاء إعانة لا أن تعيين القاضي مثبت لها وكذا بقية المسائل الخلافية، ولم يظهر لي الموجب لفرارهم من والأخ الكبير يملك ذلك إذا كان الصغير في حجره وإلا فلا فيصير حاصل الجواب أنه إذا كان طعاماً ينفق سواء كان في حجره أو لا، وإن كان دراهم إن كان في حجره يملك شراء الطعام والنفقة، وإن كان شيئاً يحتاج إلى بيعه لا يملك إلا أن يجعله القاضي وصياً، كذا في التتارخانية . قوله : (ولم يظهر لي الموجب لفرارهم من هذا قال المقدسي في شرحه أقول: لعل الموجب لفرارهم قوة الاختلاف فإذا قوي قول المخالف راعوا خلافه واستعانوا بالحكم كما في الرجوع في الهبة وخيار البلوغ وغيرهما ا هـ . وفي النهر : وأجاب تاج الشريعة بأن معنى قولهم لا تجب أي لا يجب أداؤها، أما نفس الوجوب فثابت عندنا وعلى هذا فقوله يكون إيجاباً» مبتدأ أي للأداء إلا أن مقتضاه جواز أخد شيء ظفروا به من جنس النفقة وليس كذلك فتدبر ا هـ. وقال الرملي: يجوز أن يجاب بأن معنى قولهم لا تجب أي لا تلزم إلا بالقضاء وإن كانت واجبة قبله، وقد يلزم الشيء ولا يجب كالدين اللازم ذمة المعسر لا يلزم من لزومه ذمته أدائه عليه. والفرق بين اللزوم وجوب والوجوب ظاهر وذلك للاختلاف، وقد فرقوا بين القضاء بالمتفق عليه وبين القضاء بالمختلف فيه
There an executor spent, and it was said to him that he was not appointed for that, so Muhammad recited the verse of Allah: 'And Allah knows the corrupter from the reformer' [2:220]. So what is based on this principle, there is no liability upon him between him and Allah Ta'ala by istihsan. As for the ruling, he is liable. Similarly, adult heirs if they spend on minors and there was no [appointment], they are volunteers in ruling, but in religion they are doers of good, and it is permissible for them to acknowledge what surplus from the share of the minors only, and if they swear, nothing is upon them. An analogous case: if the executor knows the debt on the deceased and pays it without acknowledging it and without the judge or heirs knowing, he does not sin. Likewise, if a man has a deposit with another, and the depositor owes a similar debt, and the depositee knows that he died and did not collect his debt, it is permissible for the depositee to consider that debt as his wealth and not approach it. Likewise, if Amr has a debt on Zayd, and on Amr a similar debt to another man, then Amr dies and Zayd knows that Amr did not pay his debt, it is permissible for Zayd to pay Amr's debt by what Amr had on Zayd and not inform his heirs. The origin of that is that Khalid took the banner and assumed authority without appointment for the sake of reform, mentioned by al-Kirmani in Sharh al-Bukhari from the chapter of funerals. And the author did not mention whether he has recourse against the one on whom he spent when he is liable? And they said: He has no recourse because the depositee owned the paid object by liability, so he was a volunteer with his own property. The apparent meaning is that there is no difference between spending on them and handing over the deposit to them in the obligation of liability and the lack of recourse against them due to the existence of the cause in both. And I have not seen that if he spent on them without order, then the owner gave permission, it is apparent that there is no liability because permission is a discharge from liability, and due to their saying: Subsequent permission is like prior agency. ... Ibn al-Walid said: Look. His saying: (And if they both spend what they have) i.e., no liability on them because they have taken their due, as their maintenance is obligatory before the court order as previously mentioned, and they have taken the same type of right. And in al-Khulasa: If a father spent from his son's property on himself, then the son contested him, saying 'You spent while you were wealthy,' and the father said 'I spent while I was insolvent,' then the situation of the father on the day of contestation is considered: if he is insolvent, his word is accepted by istihsan for the maintenance of his like; if he is wealthy, the son's word is accepted. If both present evidence, the son's evidence is preferred. And the ruling for the wife and child is like the parents: if they spend what they have, no liability, unlike other mahram relatives who are unable; they become liable by spending without court order or consent. He said in al-Dhakhira: The maintenance of parents, children, and wife is obligatory before court order, so if any of these gets hold of the same type of their right, they may take it without court order or consent. His saying: (And likewise adult heirs...) was mentioned in Nafaqat al-Khassaf: The elder brother with the younger brother, if they inherit property and there is a judge in the town or not, and the elder brother spends from the younger brother's share, he is liable in ruling because he has no authority over him. And I wrote at the end of Karahiyya of al-Jami' al-Saghir what indicates that he has the right to spend. So it is possible that the interpretation of what is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir is spending from the same type of maintenance, like food and so on, and in this there is no need for the brother to sell. And it is possible that the brother is under his guardianship and the property is dirhams and he needs to purchase what is necessary, i.e., maintenance. They both have, so if the judge decrees maintenance for children and relatives and a period passes, it lapses, unless the judge permits borrowing. For his slave, if he refuses, then from his earnings, otherwise he orders him to sell. Consent. As for the maintenance of other relatives, it is not obligatory except by court order or consent, so even if any of the relatives gets hold of the same type of his right, he may not take it except by court order or consent. Hence, the judge prescribes for the absent person's property the maintenance of only the first two. End. His saying: (And if he decrees maintenance for children and relatives and a period passes, it lapses) because the maintenance of these is obligatory as sufficiency for need, so it is not obligatory when there is wealth, and sufficiency has been achieved by the passage of time, unlike the maintenance of the wife if the judge decrees it, because it is obligatory even when she is wealthy, so it does not lapse by mere sufficiency in the past. And I have not seen that he sins; the requirement of its obligation is that he sins by abandoning it when the claimant asks and he refuses, even though they said it is not obligatory except by court order or consent as we reported from al-Dhakhira. Hence, al-Khassaf in Adab al-Qadi does not say that it is obligatory for him to take it without court order or consent, and he stated explicitly... for whom it is... he stated... maintenance except by court order due to disagreement about it. Al-Saruji in al-Ghaya questioned it, because they made the judge himself the one who imposed this maintenance, and the judge is not a legislator; that is only for the Prophet (peace be upon him) and ceased after him, so it is very problematic. Al-Tarsusi in Anfa' al-Wasa'il followed him and said: Why not? It was said: The obligation is established by His verse: 'And on the heir is similar' [2:233]; so the judge's decree is assistance for him as in the maintenance of children. Moreover, they have used this verse as evidence in the original issue for the obligation of relatives, and the word 'upon' indicates obligation. This is not affected by the disagreement of scholars, because in disputed issues, one acts according to the disagreement, and the disagreement does not affect non-acceptance, because that was obligatory before the decree, as we said about the maintenance of the irrevocably divorced woman: it is decreed considering that it is established before the decree, and the decree is assistance, not that the judge's determination is what establishes it. And likewise the rest of the disputed issues. And it has not become clear to me the reason for their avoidance of this. And the elder brother has that right if the minor is under his guardianship; otherwise not. So the summary of the answer is: if it is food, he may spend whether the minor is under his guardianship or not; if it is dirhams, if the minor is under his guardianship, he may purchase food and maintenance; if it is something that requires sale, he may not do so unless the judge makes him a guardian. This is in al-Tatarkhaniyya. His saying: (And it has not become clear to me the reason for their avoidance of this). Al-Maqdisi in his commentary said: I say: Perhaps the reason for their avoidance is the strength of the disagreement; when the opponent's view is strong, they take into account his disagreement and seek assistance from the ruling, as in the case of revocation of gift and option of puberty, etc. In al-Nahr: Taj al-Shari'a answered that the meaning of their saying 'it is not obligatory' is that its performance is not obligatory; as for the essence of obligation, it is established with us. According to this, his saying 'it becomes obligatory' means for performance, except that its requirement is permissibility of taking something they get hold of from the same type of maintenance, and that is not the case, so reflect. Al-Ramli said: It is possible to answer that the meaning of their saying 'it is not obligatory' is that it is not binding except by court order, even though it is obligatory before it. A thing may be binding but not obligatory, like the debt binding the liability of an insolvent person; the binding of his liability does not require its performance upon him. And the difference between binding and obligation is apparent. That is due to the disagreement, and they distinguished between a decree in agreed-upon matters and a decree in disputed matters.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on the rulings of spending on dependents without a court order and the necessity of a judge's decree for maintenance of certain relatives. The commentary covers multiple scenarios and legal distinctions. First, it discusses an executor who spends from the deceased's estate without being explicitly appointed: legally he is liable (damān), but religiously (diyānatan) there is no sin because he acted on the basis of istihsan, following the qiyās of the verse 'Allah knows the corrupter from the reformer'. This distinction between hukmī (legal) and dīnī (religious) liability is fundamental in Hanafi fiqh. Similarly, adult heirs spending on minor heirs: legally they are considered volunteers and liable, but religiously they are doers of good and not sinful. This applies when they spend from the exact same type of their due. Then the text gives analogous cases: a depositary paying off a debt of the depositor without court order or knowledge of heirs; a debtor paying off another's debt from what is owed to him. All these rely on the principle that if one takes the same type of their rightful due (jins al-haqq), no liability attaches. The commentary then addresses a question: does the one who spent have recourse against the recipient when he is legally liable? The answer is no, because by becoming liable he owns the property he paid, so he is a volunteer. There is no difference between spending and handing over a deposit in this regard. If the owner later ratifies the spending, liability is removed because subsequent permission is like prior agency. Next, the commentary explains the matn 'ولو أنفقا ما عندهما' (if they both spend what they have) referring to parents and children: no liability because they have taken their due, as their maintenance is obligatory before any court order. This is confirmed by a quote from al-Khulasa about a father and son dispute over spending. The ruling extends to the wife and children: they may take their due without court order, but other mahram relatives who are in need cannot; they become liable if they spend without court order or consent. This is because the maintenance of parents, children, and spouse is wajib before the qada', while for other relatives it only becomes obligatory upon court order or consent. The text then discusses the case of an elder brother spending on a younger brother: he is liable in ruling if he is not a guardian, but there is nuance depending on whether the younger is in his care and whether the property is food or cash. The commentary then addresses a key point: if a judge decrees maintenance for children and relatives and a period passes, the obligation lapses because it is need-based; but for the wife, it does not lapse even if she is wealthy because it is not merely need-based. A problematic issue arises: scholars said maintenance of relatives only becomes obligatory by court order despite it being wajib in principle. This is because of ikhtilāf (disagreement) among the schools. Some later scholars like al-Saruji found it problematic that the judge's decree creates obligation when the judge is not a legislator. The response is that the obligation is already established by the verse 'Wa 'ala al-wārithi mithlu dhālik' (2:233); the judge's decree is merely assistance (i'ānah) to enforce it, similar to maintenance of children. The disagreement does not affect the underlying obligation. Finally, the commentary resolves the tension by distinguishing between wujūb (obligation in essence) and luzūm (binding performance). The obligation of maintenance for relatives exists before the decree, but it is not binding (lāzim) for performance until the judge decrees or the recipient consents, due to the disagreement. This is similar to a debt owed by an insolvent person: the debt is binding on the liability but not due for performance. Thus, the practical difference is that one cannot take it without court order even if one gets hold of the same type. This passage is a masterful example of Hanafi legal reasoning, balancing dalīl, istihsan, and the reality of ikhtilāf.

Key Terms
ضمان (damān): Legal liability to compensate for property consumed or damaged without right; also responsibility for another's maintenance.
ديانة (diyānatan): In religious terms; the inner state before Allah, as opposed to legal enforcement.
حكماً (ḥukman): Legally; in terms of court ruling and outward enforcement.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and shelter, obligatory upon a person for certain dependents.
إجازة (ijāzah): Subsequent ratification or permission by the owner, which retroactively validates an act.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference, a method of departing from strict qiyas for a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following categories of persons may take their maintenance from the property of the obligated party without prior court order or consent?
A) A needy paternal uncle
B) A wife
C) A brother not under guardianship
D) A distant cousin

Key Takeaway
Immediate family (parents, children, spouse) may take maintenance without court order because their nafaqah is wajib before any decree; other relatives require court order or consent, as the obligation is not binding (lāzim) without it due to ikhtilaf.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
8 min
Maintenance of Children and Relatives: Why the Judge's Decree Matters
Commentary on the ruling that nafaqa of children and relatives drops after time unless judge permits borrowing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqa of children and relatives after a judge's decree and explain the role of the judge's permission in preserving the obligation.

Core Idea
Nafaqa of children and relatives drops after time unless judge permits borrowing – judicial permission converts it into a debt.

This lesson examines a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school about the nature of maintenance obligations for family members. We will explore why a judge's decree is required for relatives, how the passage of time can extinguish the duty, and the exceptional role of the judge's permission to borrow. The source passage is from Ibn Nujaym's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is nafaqa? For whom is it discussed – children and relatives? What is the basic ruling when a judge decrees it?
Nafaqa is financial support. The passage discusses what happens after a judge's decree if time passes without collection.
2
Locate the condition for the drop
The maintenance drops after a period passes. Why does it drop? What is the rationale?
It drops because it is wajib for sufficiency (kifayah); once the need is met over time, the obligation ceases.
3
Trace the exception – judge's permission to borrow
The drop does not occur if the judge permits borrowing. How does this preserve the obligation?
The permission turns the maintenance into a debt in the obligor's liability.
4
Examine the conceptual difficulty about the judge's role
Why does the Hanafi school require a judge's decree for relatives? How is this reconciled with the Quranic evidence?
The obligation exists from the Quran, but due to ikhtilaf, the judge's decree makes it enforceable.
5
Distinguish between categories of dependents
How is maintenance of children different from other relatives? How is it different from a wife's maintenance?
Children's maintenance is unconditional but still drops with time; a wife's does not drop because it is wajib even if she is wealthy.

عندهما لا فلو قضى بنفقة الولاد والقريب ومضت مدة سقطت إلا أن يأذن القاضي بالاستدانة ولمملوكه فإن أبى ففي كسبه وإلا أمره ببيعه. رضا. فأما نفقة سائر الأقارب لا تجب إلا بالقضاء أو الرضا حتى لو ظفروا واحد من الأقارب بجنس حقه لم يكن له الأخذ إلا بقضاء أو رضا، ولذا يفرض القاضي في مال الغائب نفقة الأولين فقط. اهـ. قوله: ( ولو قضى بنفقة الولاد والقريب ومضت مدة سقطت) لأن نفقة هؤلاء تجب كفاية للحاجة حتى لا تجب مع اليسار وقد حصلت الكفاية بمضي المدة بخلاف نفقة الزوجة إذا قضى بها القاضي لأنها تجب مع يسارها فلا تسقط بحصول الاستغناء فيما مضى ولم أر بأنه يأثم ومقتضى وجوبها أنه يأثم بتركها إذا طلبها صاحبها وامتنع مع أنهم قالوا إنها لا تجب إلا بالقضاء أو الرضا كما قدمناه عن الذخيرة، ولذا ليس الخصاف في أدب القاضي بأنها لا تجب له أن يأخذها بغير قضاء ولا رضا وصرح لمن هي من صرح نفقة إلا بالقضاء للاختلاف فيها واستشكله السروجي في الغاية من حيث إنهم جعلوا القاضي نفسه هو الذي أوجب هذه النفقة والقاضي ليس بمشرع وما ذاك إلا للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وانقطع من بعده فهو مشكل جداً. وتبعه على ذلك الطرسوسي في أنفع الوسائل وقال: لم لا؟ قيل: إن الوجوب يثبت بقوله تعالى: {وعلى الوارث مثل ذلك} فقضاء القاضي إعانة له كما في نفقة الأولاد، كيف وإنهم قد استدلوا في أصل المسألة بهذه الآية على وجوب القريب وكلمة «على» للإيجاب ولا يعكر على هذا اختلاف العلماء لأن المسائل الاختلافية يعمل فيها على الاختلاف ولا يكون الاختلاف مؤثراً في عدم القبول فإن ذلك كان واجباً قبل القضاء كما قلنا في نفقة المبتوتة أنه يقضي بها باعتبار أنها ثابتة قبل القضاء والقضاء إعانة لا أن تعيين القاضي مثبت لها وكذا بقية المسائل الخلافية، ولم يظهر لي الموجب لفرارهم من هذا؟ قال المقدسي في شرحه: أقول: لعل الموجب لفرارهم قوة الاختلاف فإذا قوي قول المخالف راعوا خلافه واستعانوا بالحكم كما في الرجوع في الهبة وخيار البلوغ وغيرهما. ا هـ. وفي النهر: وأجاب تاج الشريعة بأن معنى قولهم لا تجب أي لا يجب أداؤها، أما نفس الوجوب فثابت عندنا وعلى هذا فقوله يكون إيجاباً مبتدأ أي للأداء إلا أن مقتضاه جواز أخذ شيء ظفروا به من جنس النفقة وليس كذلك فتدبر. ا هـ. وقال الرملي: يجوز أن يجاب بأن معنى قولهم لا تجب أي لا تلزم إلا بالقضاء وإن كانت واجبة قبله، وقد يلزم الشيء ولا يجب كالدين اللازم ذمة المعسر لا يلزم من لزومه ذمته أدائه عليه. والفرق بين اللزوم والوجوب ظاهر وذلك للاختلاف، وقد فرقوا بين القضاء بالمتفق عليه وبين القضاء بالمختلف فيه. هذا اهـ. وفي البدائع أن شرط وجوب نفقة القريب الطلب والخصومة بين يدي القاضي في نفقة غير الولاد فلا تجب بدونه لأنها لا تجب بدون قضاء القاضي والقضاء لا بد له من الطلب والخصومة. اهـ. وهو صريح في أن الطلب من غير أن يكون بين يدي القاضي لا يكون موجباً. وأطلق المصنف في المدة وهي مقيدة بالكثيرة، أما القليلة فلا تسقط دون الشهر كما ذكره في الذخيرة وتبعها الشارحون لأنها لو سقطت بالمدة اليسيرة لما أمكنهم استيفاؤها. وفي فتح القدير: وكيف لا تصير القصيرة ديناً والقاضي مأمور بالقضاء، ولو لم تصر ديناً لم يكن بالأمر بالقضاء فائدة، ولو كان كلما مضى سقط لم يمكن استيفاء شيء ومثل هذا قدمناه في غير المفروضة من نفقات الزوجات. اهـ. وأطلق في نفقة الولاد فشمل الأصول والفروع الصغار والكبار واستثنى في الذخيرة معزياً إلى الحاوي وأقره عليه الزيلعي نفقة الصغير فإنها تصير عليه ديناً على الأب بقضاء القاضي بخلاف نفقة سائر الأقارب. وفي الواقعات: وإذا فرض نفقة الأب أو الابن فلم يقبض سنين ثم أيسر أو مات تبطل لأن هذا يصير دينا من كل وجه. اهـ. ولا يخفى أن تعليق البطلان على اليسار أو الموت ليس بقيد لما ذكرناه. قوله: (إلا أن يأذن القاضي بالاستدانة) فلا تسقط يعني بمضي المدة لأن القاضي له ولاية عامة فصار إذنه كأمر الغائب فتصير ديناً في ذمته، وقد أخل المصنف بقيد لا بد منه الاستدانة والإنفاق مما استدانة كما قيده في المبسوط والنهاية وغيرهما حتى قال الطرسوسي: ولقد غلط بعض الفقهاء هنا في مفهوم كلام صاحب الهداية وقال: إذا أذن القاضي في الاستدانة ولم يستدن فإنها لا تسقط وهذا غلط بل معنى الكلام إذن القاضي في الاستدانة واستدان وهذا يفيد أن القيد المتروك هو الاستدانة بعد الأمر بها لا الإنفاق مما استدان. وفي
According to them, no; if he decreed maintenance for children and a relative and a period passed, it drops unless the judge permits borrowing. And for his slave, if he refuses, then from his earnings, otherwise he orders him to be sold. Consent: As for maintenance of other relatives, it is not obligatory except by decree or consent, so even if they find something from a relative of the same kind as his right, he cannot take it except by decree or consent. Therefore, the judge imposes maintenance for only the first two from the wealth of the absent. End. His statement: (And if he decreed maintenance for children and a relative and a period passed, it drops) because the maintenance of these is obligatory for sufficiency of need, so it is not obligatory with wealth, and sufficiency has been obtained by the passage of time, unlike the maintenance of the wife if the judge decrees it because it is obligatory even if she is wealthy, so it does not drop by becoming self-sufficient in the past. And I did not see that he sins... and the implication of its obligation is that he sins by abandoning it when its owner requests and he refuses, despite them saying it is not obligatory except by decree or consent as we presented from al-Dhakhira. Therefore, al-Khasaf in Adab al-Qadi says it is not obligatory for him to take it without decree or consent, and he clarified for whom? He clarified maintenance except by decree due to ikhtilaf. Al-Saruji in al-Ghaya found this problematic because they made the judge himself the one who obligated this maintenance, and the judge is not a legislator; that is only for the Prophet, peace be upon him, and ceased after him, so it is very problematic. And al-Tarsusi in Anfa' al-Wasa'il followed him and said: why not? It was said: The obligation is established by His statement: {And upon the heir is the like of that} so the judge's decree is assistance for him as in maintenance of children. How? And they have used this verse as evidence for the obligation of the relative, and the word 'upon' indicates obligation. The difference of scholars does not impair this because in cases of ikhtilaf, one acts according to the ikhtilaf, and ikhtilaf does not affect non-acceptance, for that was obligatory before the decree as we said in the maintenance of the abandoned wife that he decrees it on the basis that it is established before the decree, and the decree is assistance, not that the judge's designation establishes it. Similarly for other matters of khilaf. And I have not seen the reason for their fleeing from this. Al-Maqdisi in his commentary said: I say: perhaps the reason for their fleeing is the strength of the ikhtilaf, so when the opponent's view is strong, they observe his opposition and seek help from the ruling as in the case of returning a gift and the option of puberty etc. End. In al-Nahr: Taj al-Shari'ah answered that the meaning of their saying 'it is not obligatory' means its performance is not obligatory, but the obligation itself is established according to us. And accordingly, his saying would be an obligation initiator i.e. for performance, except that its implication is the permissibility of taking something they find of the same kind as maintenance, and that is not so, so ponder. End. And al-Ramli said: It is possible to answer that the meaning of their saying 'it is not obligatory' means it is not binding except by decree, even though it was obligatory before it. And a thing may be binding but not obligatory, like a debt binding on the insolvent's liability, but his liability does not require its payment. The difference between binding and obligation is clear, and that is due to ikhtilaf. And they distinguished between decree in agreed upon matters and decree in disputed matters. End. In al-Bada'i': The condition for the obligation of maintenance of a relative is the demand and litigation before the judge in maintenance other than children, so it is not obligatory without it because it is not obligatory without the judge's decree, and the decree necessarily requires demand and litigation. And the author made the period absolute, but it is restricted to a long period; as for a short period, it does not drop, less than a month as mentioned in al-Dhakhira and followed by the commentators, because if it dropped in a short period, they could not recover it. And in Fath al-Qadir: How does a short period not become a debt while the judge is commanded to decree? If it did not become a debt, there would be no benefit in the command to decree. If every time a period passed it dropped, nothing could be recovered. Similar to this we presented in non-fixed maintenance of wives. End. And he made absolute in maintenance of children, so it includes ascendants and descendants, minors and adults. In al-Waqi'at: If maintenance of a father or son is imposed, and he does not receive it for years, then becomes wealthy or dies, it is void because this becomes a debt from every aspect. End. It is not hidden that tying invalidity to wealth or death is not a restriction for what we mentioned. His statement: (Except that the judge permits borrowing) so it does not drop meaning by passage of time, because the judge has general authority, so his permission is like the order of an absent person, so it becomes a debt in his liability. And the author omitted a necessary condition: borrowing and spending from what was borrowed. As he restricted it in al-Mabsut and al-Nihaya and others, such that al-Tarsusi said: Some jurists have erred here in understanding the statement of the author of al-Hidayah, and said: If the judge permits borrowing but he does not borrow, it does not drop. This is erroneous. Rather the meaning of the statement is permission of the judge to borrow and he borrows. This implies that the omitted condition is borrowing after the order, not spending from what was borrowed. And in...
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding maintenance of children and relatives. The author (Ibn Nujaym) explains that if a judge decrees maintenance for children (awlād) or a relative (qarīb) and a period of time passes without collection, the maintenance drops (saqata) and is no longer owed. This is because such maintenance is wajib for sufficiency (kifāyah) – to meet the need – and once the need is fulfilled by the passing of time, the obligation no longer applies. However, this is contrasted with the maintenance of a wife: a wife's maintenance is wajib even if she is wealthy, so it does not drop with time. The exception to this rule is if the judge permits the recipient to borrow (istidānah). In that case, the maintenance does not drop and becomes a debt.

The author then addresses a conceptual problem: the Hanafi school states that maintenance of relatives (other than children) is not obligatory (lā tajib) except by a judge's decree or mutual consent. This seems paradoxical because the judge is not a legislator; only the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) could obligate. The answer is that the obligation itself is established by the Quranic verse (2:233) 'And upon the heir is the like of that,' but due to ikhtilāf among scholars, the judge's decree removes the doubt and makes the obligation enforceable. Thus, the judge's decree serves as assistance (i'ānah), not as a source of obligation.

The text also clarifies the conditions: the period that leads to dropping must be long (kathīrah); a short period (less than a month) does not cause dropping. And if the judge permits borrowing, the maintenance does not drop, but this requires both permission and actual borrowing (istidānah wa istidān). Some scholars erred in understanding al-Hidāyah by thinking that mere permission suffices.

There is an exception for the maintenance of a minor child: it becomes a debt on the father by the judge's decree, unlike other relatives. Similarly, maintenance of a father or son, if not collected for years, becomes void if the obligor becomes wealthy or dies.

This passage is an expansion probe commentary, providing detailed reasoning and addressing conceptual difficulties.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqa): Maintenance, financial support for food, clothing, and lodging.
قضاء (qadā'): A judge's legal decree or judgment.
استدانة (istidānah): Borrowing money from someone.
اختلاف (ikhtilāf): Disagreement among scholars on a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does maintenance of a child or relative drop after a judge's decree?
A) It never drops.
B) It drops after a long period unless the judge permitted borrowing and actual borrowing occurred.
C) It drops after any period automatically.
D) It drops only if the relative becomes self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, maintenance of children and relatives after a judge's decree drops with the passage of time unless the judge's permission to borrow and actual borrowing convert it into a debt.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Condition of Demand and Time in Nafaqa of Relatives
Explains the requirement of demand and litigation for nafaqa of non-child relatives, and the effect of time passage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which nafaqa for non-child relatives becomes a binding debt and distinguish the effect of short vs long time passage.

Core Idea
Nafaqa for non-child relatives requires demand and litigation; time drops debt only after a month.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents two key rulings on maintenance (nafaqa) for relatives: first, that a formal demand and litigation before a judge are required for the nafaqa of relatives other than children to become an enforceable debt; second, that the passage of time only cancels the debt if it exceeds one month. The text also discusses an exception for minor children and a scenario where prolonged non-collection voids the debt entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? Nafaqa for relatives. What is the hukm level? Wajib when conditions met. Here the focus is on when it becomes a debt (dayn).
2
Locate the conditions for debt creation
For non-child relatives, the text states that demand (talab) and litigation (khusuma) before a judge are necessary. Without them, no obligation arises.
Al-Badai'i is cited as the source.
3
Analyze the time effect
The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq made mudda (time) unrestricted, but the commentators restrict it: only a long period (more than a month) cancels the debt. A short period does not, lest the beneficiary be unable to collect.
Support from al-Dhakhira and Fath al-Qadir.
4
Note the exception for children
Nafaqa of children includes all ascendants and descendants, but for a minor child, it becomes a debt by the judge's decree, unlike other relatives. This is an exception from al-Hawi.
5
Examine the voiding scenario
If nafaqa for a father or son is fixed but uncollected for years, then the payer becomes rich or dies, the debt becomes void because it is a debt from every direction (i.e., not tied to a specific time).
From al-Waqi'at.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما وهي هذا اهـ. وفي البدائع أن شرط وجوب نفقة القريب الطلب والخصومة بين يدي القاضي في نفقة غير الولاد فلا تجب بدونه لأنها لا تجب بدون قضاء القاضي والقضاء لا بد له من الطلب والخصومة .اهـ. وهو صريح في أن الطلب من غير أن يكون بين يدي القاضي لا يكون موجباً. وأطلق المصنف في المدة وهي مقيدة بالكثيرة، أما القليلة فلا تسقط دون الشهر كما ذكره في الذخيرة وتبعها الشارحون لأنها لو سقطت بالمدة اليسيرة لما أمكنهم استيفاؤها. وفي فتح القدير : وكيف لا تصير القصيرة ديناً والقاضي مأمور بالقضاء، ولو لم تصر ديناً لم يكن بالأمر بالقضاء فائدة، ولو كان كلما مضى سقط لم يمكن استيفاء شيء ومثل هذا قدمناه في غير المفروضة من نفقات الزوجات .اهـ وأطلق في نفقة الولاد فشمل الأصول والفروع الصغار والكبار واستثنى في الذخيرة معزياً إلى الحاوي وأقره عليه الزيلعي نفقة الصغير فإنها تصير عليه ديناً على الأب بقضاء القاضي بخلاف نفقة سائر الأقارب. وفي الواقعات : وإذا فرض نفقة الأب أو الابن فلم يقبض سنين ثم أيسر أو مات تبطل لأن هذا يصير دينا من كل وجه .اهـ. ولا يخفى أن تعليق البطلان على اليسار أو الموت ليس بقيد لما ذكرناه
And this is the end. In al-Badai'i: the condition for obligation of maintenance of a relative is demand and litigation before the judge for maintenance other than for children, so it is not obligatory without it, because it is not obligatory without a judge's decree, and a decree must have demand and litigation. This is explicit that demand without being before the judge does not make it binding. The author [of Kanz] made mudda unrestricted, but it is restricted to a long period; a short period does not drop it, less than a month, as mentioned in al-Dhakhira and followed by the commentators, because if it dropped with a short period, they would not be able to collect it. In Fath al-Qadir: how would a short period not become a debt while the judge is commanded to decree; if it did not become a debt, there would be no benefit in the command to decree, and if every time that passed it dropped, nothing could be collected. We have mentioned similar in non-prescribed expenses of wives. And he [the author] made mudda unrestricted in the nafaqa of children, so it includes ascendants and descendants, young and old. An exception was made in al-Dhakhira, attributed to al-Hawi and confirmed by al-Zayla'i, for nafaqa of a minor, for it becomes a debt on the father by the judge's decree, unlike nafaqa of other relatives. In al-Waqi'at: if the nafaqa of a father or son is fixed and he does not collect for years, then he becomes wealthy or dies, it becomes void because this becomes a debt from every aspect. It is not hidden that conditioning voidance on becoming wealthy or death is not a restriction for what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the conditions under which maintenance (nafaqa) for relatives becomes a binding debt. The first ruling states that for relatives other than children, the obligation of nafaqa requires two things: demand (talab) and litigation (khusuma) before a judge. Without these, no debt is created. The reasoning is that the judge's decree is necessary, and that decree itself requires a formal demand and dispute. The second ruling addresses the effect of time (mudda). The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq stated that nafaqa drops with the passage of time, but this is qualified: only a long time causes it to drop, not a short time (less than a month). This is from al-Dhakhira, supported by Fath al-Qadir, to prevent the beneficiary from being unable to collect because of frequent small intervals. The third ruling distinguishes nafaqa of children (al-walad): it includes all ascendants and descendants, young and old. However, there is an exception from al-Dhakhira that for a minor child, the nafaqa becomes a debt on the father only by the judge's decree, unlike other relatives. The final ruling from al-Waqi'at concerns a scenario where maintenance for a father or son is fixed but not collected for years; if the payer later becomes wealthy or dies, the debt becomes void because it is a debt from every direction (i.e., it is not tied to a specific time). This clarifies that such voidance is not limited to those conditions but is general.

Key Terms
الطلب (al-talab): Formal demand for maintenance made to the judge or the obliged party.
الخصومة (al-khusuma): Litigation or legal dispute before a judge to establish the obligation.
المدة (al-mudda): The period of time that passes without payment of nafaqa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for nafaqa of a relative (other than children) to become a binding debt?
A) Mere passage of time
B) Demand and litigation before a judge
C) A verbal agreement between parties
D) Written documentation

Key Takeaway
Nafaqa for non-child relatives requires formal demand and litigation to become a debt; short time (< 1 month) does not cancel it, but long time does.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate

Actual Borrowing Required After Judge's Permission
Critical refinement of a borrowing permission rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that actual borrowing is necessary after the judge's permission for nafaqa to remain a debt that does not drop with time.

Core Idea
Qadi's permission enables borrowing; only actual borrowing creates a debt that survives time.

A subtle point in Kanz al-Daqa'iq can be misunderstood: the judge's permission to borrow for nafaqa does not by itself prevent the nafaqa from dropping. This lesson clarifies that actual borrowing and spending the borrowed amount are essential. We trace the correction from al-Tarsusi and the broader Hanafi textual tradition.

Guided Path
1
State the ambiguous ruling
Identify what Kanz al-Daqa'iq says: nafaqa does not drop with time if the judge permits borrowing. Recognize the ambiguity: does permission alone suffice?
2
Locate the correction
Note that al-Tarsusi explicitly corrects those who thought permission alone is enough. He asserts that the condition 'actual borrowing' is implied, as stated in al-Mabsut and al-Nihaya.
3
Understand the rationale
Analyze why permission alone does not create a debt: the judge's permission is like an order to an absent person, but without actual borrowing, there is no demonstration of need, and spending one's own money or begging does not generate a claim.
4
Apply the corrected ruling to scenarios
Test the ruling: if the guardian borrows after permission, the debt stands and does not drop if the liable person dies. If he spends his own money or the child begs, no recourse exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا أن يأذن القاضي بالاستدانة) فلا تسقط يعني بمضي المدة لأن القاضي له ولاية عامة فصار إذنه كأمر الغائب فتصير ديناً في ذمته، وقد أخل المصنف بقيد لا بد منه الاستدانة والإنفاق مما استدانة كما قيده في المبسوط والنهاية وغيرهما حتى قال الطرسوسي : ولقد غلط بعض الفقهاء هنا في مفهوم كلام صاحب الهداية وقال : إذا أذن القاضي في الاستدانة ولم يستدن فإنها لا تسقط وهذا غلط بل معنى الكلام إذن القاضي في فالأول يعمل فيما سبق وفيما لحق كالقضاء بأن فلاناً من ذرية الواقف لأنه كاشف. والثاني لا يعمل فيما مضى ويعمل فيما يستقبل كالقضاء بدخول أولاد البنات في الوقف على أولاد الأولاد بعد مضي سنين وكذا في كثير His statement: 'Except that the judge permits borrowing' means it does not drop with the passage of time, because the judge has general authority, so his permission is like the order of an absent one, so it becomes a debt in his liability. The author omitted an essential condition: borrowing and spending from what is borrowed, as qualified in al-Mabsut, al-Nihaya, and others. Until al-Tarsusi said: 'Some jurists erred in understanding the statement of the author of al-Hidayah and said: if the judge permits borrowing and he does not borrow, then it does not drop, but this is an error. Rather, the meaning of the statement is the judge's permission in... The first [type of qada] operates on what has passed and what will come, like the judgment that so-and-so is from the descendants of the waqif, because it is revealing. The second [type] does not operate on the past but operates on the future, like the judgment admitting the daughters' children into the waqf on the children's children after the passage of years. Similarly in many branches. If the disputed and the agreed were equal, their distinction would not be valid. So the judgment on disputed matters makes it agreed. The noble verse is subject to interpretation... It is similar to the answer of al-Maqdisi. His statement: 'And an exception was made from al-Dhakhira...' I say: what the author mentions later about al-Dhakhira contradicts this exception, ponder. It is apparent that the author was not satisfied with this exception, ponder. His statement: 'Bal' meaning the judge's permission to borrow and he borrowed indicates that the omitted condition is borrowing after the order, not spending from what is borrowed. Regarding borrowing and he borrowed: end. He said in al-Mabsut: if he spends after permission to borrow from his own money or from sadaqa given to him, he has no recourse because there is no need. And it is stated explicitly in al-Dhakhira regarding nafaqa of minor children: if they eat from begging, their mother has no recourse against the father for anything. But if they were given half sufficiency and the mother borrowed for them the other half, she can recover what she borrowed, as we have mentioned. The author indicated that it does not drop after the permitted borrowing: if the one liable for nafaqa dies after that, it does not drop according to the correct position, but is taken from his estate, and that debt then prevents zakat because it is a debt demanded by people. In al-Khaniyya: a man was absent and did not leave nafaqa for his minor children, and their mother had wealth; the mother is compelled to spend and then she recovers that from the husband. He did not require borrowing nor permission for it, so there is a distinction between when she spends from her own money and when they beg. In al-Bazzaziyya: the mother said to the judge...
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a misunderstanding regarding the Hidayah's statement about the judge's permission for borrowing. The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq states: 'except that the judge permits borrowing (istidana),' meaning that nafaqa does not drop with time if the judge permits borrowing. The commentator explains that the judge's permission makes the nafaqa a debt in the payer's liability because the judge has general authority. However, the author of Kanz omitted an essential condition: that actual borrowing and spending from the borrowed amount must occur, as qualified in al-Mabsut, al-Nihaya, and others. Al-Tarsusi corrected a mistake by some jurists who thought that the permission alone suffices. He clarifies that the meaning is permission to borrow AND the person actually borrows. He then distinguishes between two types of qada: one that is declarative (kashif) and works retrospectively, and one that is constitutive and works prospectively. The judge's permission for borrowing is the latter type. The passage then provides various scenarios: if after permission the person spends his own money or sadaqa, he has no recourse because there was no need to borrow. For minor children, if they beg, the mother cannot recover; but if she borrows to supplement, she can. If the liable person dies, the debt does not drop but is taken from the estate. In al-Khaniyya, a husband's absence forces the mother to spend from her own money, and she can recover without the condition of borrowing or permission. Finally, al-Bazzaziyya mentions the mother asking the judge (text cuts off).

Key Terms
الاستدانة (al-istidana): The act of borrowing money to cover nafaqa expenses.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission from the judge to the guardian or mother to borrow for nafaqa.
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The right to claim repayment from the person liable for nafaqa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the corrected Hanafi position, what is necessary for nafaqa to not drop with time after a judge's permission?
A) The judge's permission to borrow
B) The judge's permission and actual borrowing
C) Two witnesses to the permission
D) A written contract of debt

Key Takeaway
A judge's permission to borrow only enables the debt; actual borrowing is the operative act that prevents nafaqa from dropping with time.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
6 min
Mother's Recourse for Child Maintenance: Borrowing vs. Voluntary Spending
Ruling on mother's recourse after spending on children

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mother has a right of recourse against the father for child maintenance, and distinguish between recourse from borrowing versus voluntary spending.

Core Idea
Only actual borrowing with need creates a debt; voluntary spending is a gift, not a loan.

Maintenance of young children is the father's wajib duty, but what happens if the mother spends on them instead? Does she get her money back? The Hanafi school draws a sharp line: if she spends from her own wealth or from charity, she has no recourse—it is considered a gift. Only if she actually borrows (istidānah) with the permission of the judge or father, creating a real need, does she acquire a claim. This ruling shows the school's careful attention to the effective cause ('illa) of need and the nature of debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the mother spending on her young children. Determine the legal status of her recourse (rujūʿ): is it wajib or merely permissible? The passage treats it as a valid claim only under specific conditions.
Recourse is not automatic; it depends on the mode of spending.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The passage does not cite a primary text but uses analogy (qiyas) and the principle of need (hajah). The implied proof is that actual borrowing creates a real debt analogous to the father borrowing himself, whereas voluntary spending is akin to a gift.
The dalil is rational and derived from the concept of need.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
The effective cause ('illa) for recourse is need (hajah). When the mother borrows, she incurs a liability, proving genuine need. When she spends from her own wealth, no need exists—she could have kept her money. Thus the link between borrowing and recourse is established.
The judge's permission (idhn) formalizes the need and authorizes the debt.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (dispute)
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Khassaf and some later sources hold that if the father dies before repayment, the mother cannot recover from his estate. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq sides with the view that the debt survives death, relying on al-Asl (Imam Muhammad).
The thamarah (practical consequence) is whether the mother gets paid after the father's death.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred position)
The text declares the survival of the debt as 'al-sahih' (the correct view). The tarjih is based on the authority of the founding Imams and the analogy to any other debt that does not lapse upon death.
Thus the mufta bihi is that the mother recovers from the estate if she borrowed with permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في المبسوط : فلو أنفق بعد الإذن بالاستدانة ماله أو من صدقة تصدق بها عليه فلا رجوع له عليه لعدم الحاجة .اهـ. وصرح في الذخيرة في نفقة الأولاد الصغار أنهم إذا أكلوا من مسألة الناس فلا رجوع لأمهم على الأب بشيء، فلو أعطوا نصف الكفاية واستدانت الأم لهم النصف رجعت بما استدانت وقد قدمناه. وأفاد المصنف بعدم سقوطها بعد الاستدانة المأذون فيها أنه لو مات من عليه النفقة بعد ذلك لا تسقط على الصحيح بل تؤخذ من تركته وأن دينها حينئذ مانع من وجوب الزكاة لأنه دین له مطالب من جهة العباد. وفي الخانية : رجل غاب ولم يترك لأولاده الصغار نفقة ولأمهم مال تجبر الأم على الإنفاق ثم ترجع ذلك على الزوج .اهـ. ولم يشترط الاستدانة ولا الإذن بها فيفرق بين إذا أنفقت عليهم من مالها وبين ما إذا أكلوا من المسألة. وفي البزازية : قالت الأم للقاضي أفرض نفقة هذا الصغير على أبيه ومرني حتى أستدين عليه ففعله القاضي، وإذا استدانت عليه وأيسر رجعت عليه فإن لم ترجع عليه حتى مات لا تأخذه من تركته في الصحيح، وإن أنفقت عليه من مالها أو من المسألة من الناس لا ترجع على الأب، وكذا في نفقة المحارم اهـ. ثم رأيت بخط بعض المعاصرين نقلاً عن المضمرات قال : وفي المضمرات في الذخيرة وإذا كان الأب عاجزاً عن الكسب ولا مال له ولا للصغير ذكر الخصاف أنه يفرض القاضي النفقة على الأب، وكذا لو كان واجداً للنفقة فامتنع عن أفرض نفقة هذا الصغير على أبيه ومرني حتى أستدين عليه ففعله القاضي، وإذا استدانت عليه وأيسر رجعت عليه فإن لم ترجع عليه حتى مات لا تأخذه من تركته في الصحيح، وإن أنفقت عليه من مالها أو من المسألة من الناس لا ترجع على الأب، وكذا في نفقة المحارم اهـ.
He said in al-Mabsut: If he spent after permission to borrow from his own wealth or from charity given to him, he has no recourse because there was no need. And it is stated explicitly in al-Dhakhira regarding maintenance of young children that if they eat from begging, their mother has no recourse against the father for anything. If they were given half of sufficiency and the mother borrowed for them the half, she may recover what she borrowed, as we mentioned earlier. And the author indicated that it does not lapse after permitted borrowing: if the person obligated to maintenance dies after that, it does not lapse according to the correct view, but is taken from his estate, and her debt at that time prevents zakat because it is a debt that she can claim from others. In al-Khaniya: A man was absent and left no maintenance for his young children, and their mother had wealth; the mother is compelled to spend, then she recovers that from the husband. He did not stipulate borrowing or permission for it; so there is a distinction between when she spends on them from her own wealth and when they eat from begging. In al-Bazzaziyya: The mother said to the judge, 'I ask you to impose maintenance for this minor on his father and order me to borrow on his behalf,' so the judge did that. If she borrows on his behalf and the father becomes wealthy, she recovers from him; but if she does not recover from him until he dies, she takes nothing from his estate according to the correct view. If she spent on him from her own wealth or from begging from people, she does not recover from the father. Similarly for maintenance of relatives. Then I saw in the handwriting of some contemporaries, quoting from al-Mudmarat: In al-Mudmarat in al-Dhakhira: If the father is unable to earn and has no wealth, nor does the minor, al-Khassaf said the judge imposes maintenance on the father. Likewise if he has the means but refuses, he says: I impose maintenance of this minor on his father and order me to borrow on his behalf; the judge does that. If she borrows on his behalf and he becomes wealthy, she recovers from him; but if she does not recover from him until he dies, she takes nothing from his estate according to the correct view. If she spent on him from her own wealth or from begging from people, she does not recover from the father. Similarly for maintenance of relatives.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage from 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' discusses the detailed rulings regarding a mother's right to recoup maintenance she spends on her young children from the father. The core principle is that the right of recourse (ruju') is contingent upon the existence of a need (hajah) that necessitates borrowing. If the mother spends from her own wealth or from charity after being authorized to borrow, there is no actual need because she had the means, so no recourse. Only if she actually borrows—creating a debt—does she have a claim. The same applies if the children ate from begging: no recourse. The father's death does not extinguish the debt if it was incurred with proper authorization; it is taken from his estate. The passage also contrasts the case where the judge orders the mother to borrow (with a specific instruction) versus merely compelling her to spend from her own wealth—in the former, she recovers; in the latter, not. A distinction is made between the mother's own wealth and borrowed funds. The later citation from al-Dhakhira via al-Mudmarat confirms the rule and adds the scenario of an unable father. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that if the mother borrows with the judge's order, she recovers from the father's estate even after his death, contrary to some later opinions.

Key Terms
الاستدانة (al-istidānah): Borrowing money with the permission of the qadi or father.
الرجوع (al-rujūʿ): The right of a person who spent on another to recover that amount from the obligated person.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission from the qadi or father to borrow for maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rulings cited, what is the consequence if a mother, after being permitted by the judge to borrow for her child's maintenance, instead spends from her own wealth?
A) She can still recover because the judge's permission suffices.
B) She cannot recover because no actual borrowing occurred; the need is absent.
C) She can recover only if the father consents later.
D) She can recover from the father's estate after his death.

Key Takeaway
A mother's recourse for child maintenance in the Hanafi school requires actual borrowing with permission; voluntary spending or charity creates no claim, and the debt survives the father's death on the correct view.
